RATING: 9
I have been tiring of Dominion and picked this up as an alternative deck-builder game.  The solo variant was a huge plus for me.  So far I've played about 5 solo games and one 3-player game.  The solo game is well done and very enjoyable.  It seems the multi-player variants will be a lot o fun as well, but I should mention that the other players in my three-player game were familiar with Dominion and didn't take to this as quickly as I'd hoped.

After several more solo plays I'm upgrading this from an 8 to a 9.  I have been playing a lot of board games solo lately (Ravenloft, DungeonQuest, Runebound, Death Angel, Agricola, Arkham Horror, Ascension, etc), many of which have been fun.  Thunderstone provides the best solo experience by far, however.  In most of the other games I'm left feeling like it would be a lot more fun with someone else, but this doesn't happen with TS...it just works and is incredibly fun and challenging.

The one caveat to my rating is that I suspect I won't actually enjoy playing it with other people as much as I do solo, and might only rate a 7 or 8 over the long term as a multi-player game.

RATING: 5
Comparison with Dominion cannot be avoided. Thunderstone is essentially the very same game except for the fact that where in Dominion you only have one currency, Thunderstone boasts three. One for buying things in the village and two for buying victory points. I find this an unnecessary complication to a game in which the most interesting part is the analysis of the setup (=choosing strategy). The tactical phase in a game like this should play fast so that you can see whether your analysis was correct or not, but I find Thunderstone a bit too long. You have too many monsters to defeat before the game ends. Furthermore, the game includes a transition from village deck to dungeon deck (i.e. from one currency to another) which makes the game drag.

Thunderstone is not a bad game but Dominion is more elegant, for it plays faster and has fewer rule breaking exceptions while still manages to offer the same joy of analysing the setup, devising a strategy and executing it.

RATING: 7.3
This is great fun. A comparison to Dominion is inevitable.

Here are the differences:
-theme
-balancing 2 resources (attack/light and gold)
-Monsters vs victory cards handled a little differently. The way Thunderstone does it adds a luck factor.
-Dominion has more ways in the form of action to mitigate the luck (although it's always there). 
-Related to above: Thunderstone you are mostly stuck with what you have, which actually makes turns quicker because thinking can be done beforehand

I can see these differences mattering to some people. To me it just makes it a little different. I think if you made the themes for both games match then you're left with games that you can hardly distinguish, so I'm not sure that this game differentiates itself from Dominion enough for me to want to get it. Perhaps with expansions that's another story...

RATING: 4
Played four and five-player. There's a great of redundancy here, infecting different areas of the game. I'm surprised this was allowed, or wasn't recognised, by those responsible. This means you often can't use whats present. The numerous ruleset revisions are not encouraging. and put a question-mark against the conceptual production process, not to mention any "testing". It gets boring fast, as the only theme here seems to be whether you put a big toe, a foot or both feet round the corner of the dungeon entrance -- Dominion may not have a theme but it isn't quite so thematically limited. The game drags and I was thinking "this is crap" whilst waiting for my turn. I'm unconvinced that a "small deck" strategy is possible, since there's a great deal of lead time before a six-card hand from your deck can kill a monster, and a longer period prior to the emergence of the most powerful heroes.

The basic ideas are good and workable, so a little more know-how next time.

RATING: 4
A poor Dominion clone.  Deck-building to reach a threshold (defeat monsters for EXP and VP).  Positive reinforcement in that defeating monsters yields EXP which results in efficient upgrading of heroes and a stronger deck to more easily defeat monsters.

Negative reinforcement in that failing to meet a threshold is really, [i]really[/i] painful.  I've had games where, due to the draw or other players taking the easy ones, I just couldn't reach a threshold to take out more than 1 or 2 monsters.

In Dominion, the thresholds are obvious and the cards interact nicely to provide a number of different options.  Here, there are so many fewer choices and very few strategies available.

In Dominion, more VP cards bought means a tougher deck to play as they clog up the works.  Here, some of the VP cards have additional benefits that help you.  They're not that imposing at all.

Artwork needs improvement.  Not enough differentiation in symbols.  Theme is excellent.  Replay value is high-ish.

Honestly, there's no reason to play this over Dominion other than theme.  I don't like this one and it's a tough sell to convince me to play it.  Not for me at all, which is a shame 'cause I love the theme.

RATING: 3
[With Wrath of the Elements]

The monster luck makes this not really that much worth playing, I think.  It's not a _terrible_ game, but it goes on a good bit longer than it should, without enough interest past the early mid-game to hold you.  It's also (1) solitaire and (2) over-luck-driven.  The larger decks and more need for "multi-card combos" than in a certain other game make for higher variance in a bad sense, as well.

In short, I'm a guy so I'm not immune to the theme ("Whoa, my guys can KILL ANYTHING") but I'm with Mrs. alexd in wondering why not just play that other game.  Which we didn't even like at first!

RATING: 5.5
Like all deck building games. It's a slow start. It's even slower if the first 3 monsters are 'large'. 

The initial buys on the available village cards will determine what are the monsters you can defeat. Which eventually account for your winning in the game. 

Like most deck building games. There is minimal interaction between players. The meta game is near the end where your characters starts to be more powerful and killing monsters become easier. 

Graphics are great. Game play seem novel at first but it actually bogs the game down a few more turns especially with the 'light' component. 

Minimal entertaining value to me.

RATING: 8
I guess everyone is going to have their favorite deck building game. This one is mine. Is it the greatest game in the world? No. But it is pretty quick to play, offers plenty of variety and paths to victory while still offering the players plenty of tough choices. I find the option to have something to use your deck on (the monsters) really adds to the appeal for me. Some of those monsters are REAL tough. The combination of heroes, monsters and village cards adds plenty of variety. Some village cards are pretty good but are tempered to some extent by the heroes and monsters that are in play. Just having the best cards in your deck will not win you the game usually. You have to know how to best utilize the cards available to you. I think that this aspect makes the game more replayable.      I love the variety that the expansions add to the game. There are lots of very interesting heroes and village cards along with plenty of very cool monsters. A very solid game. Also, AEG has done a great job helping players store all the cards that come in the different expansions. It's great that I can store the base game, promos and three expansions in a relatively small box and still have room for more cards. All the cards are also very nicely organized to make set-up and tear down go quickly so you have more time to play. Hats off to AEG for that.

RATING: 4
* just like in MtG you are supposed to compile an "optimal" deck, only here you have to do it on the fly, which means it's harder for you to estimate probabilities of getting given card, plus you need to memorize which cards are still there in your deck and how many of each. 
* monster cards going into your deck are not helping at all with problem no.1
* gameplay is mostly tedious math calculations at all times (instead of dungeon crawling fun).
* apart from rogue hero sometimes discarding your opponent's cards there is literally no player interaction in the whole game. 
* constant deck shuffling takes as much time as the rest of the game.
* i didn't get it why you can't level up while resting (seems pretty obvious). getting rid of cards using draw-and-discard mechanic is a torture. 
* plenty of annoying card texts ("eliminate this-that").
* winning points system doesn't go hand in hand with dungeon crawl setting at all. final count is pretty anti-climactic. 
* time consuming setup (and sorting out messed up pile of 500 cards [if you or some other guy was not careful] can take up to an hour).
* rules are pretty badly organized. 
(в общем, понимаю, почему для игры в какой-то момент переписали правила [см. Thunderstone Advanced {нет на русском}])
upd: оказывается, нужно сначала взять карты на руку, а потом решать, куда идти. т.е. проблема но.1 стоит не так остро, как при игре "вслепую".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The game has a much stronger theme and a more interesting scoring method than its predecessor.  Because you have to choose between preparing for battle or actually battling on every turn, you must balance your deck between being strong at deckbuilding and being strong at scoring points.  The abilities of the cards are probably about as varied as the core Dominion's mix of cards, but knowing how much preparation you need before going into battle is thematically fun and strategically interesting.

Now for the part of the comment where I whine.  Parts of the game seem unpolished. Several times during our inaugural Thunderstone session, my fellow players and I just asked each other "Why is this card worded like this?" or "How is that supposed to be a balanced card?" (though we haven't played much, so this one is just a n00b's opinion). Unless my group just evaluates cards and wording differently from most people, these seem like avoidable problems.

The other problem I have with Thunderstone is the cards themselves.  First, why XP cards? They don't add anything, and I would rather just have cardboard chips.  Second, although the art is pretty good, the cards themselves are rather poor quality (many stacks were starting to warp as soon as we took the plastic off!).  If you're going to sell a card game as a big-box game with a big-box price, I'd appreciate it if you at least made sure the cards were high-quality.

I love the theme, I love the dungeon/village division, and judging on core mechanics, I'd call this game Dominion's equal.  With some higher production values and some minor tweaks for balance and wording, I would probably rate this game a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Dominion the Dungeon Crawl brings a bit more theme to the card-shuffling action.  Where Thunderstone suffers in comparison to Dominion is in a lack of interesting decisions.  It's your turn; do your six cards have the attack value to kill a monster and earn v.p.s?  If so, do it.  If not, go to the village and buy something you are short of (effectively heroes, weapons, money or light sources).  Rinse and repeat for much, much longer than a game of Dominion.

The cards are quite well made, though the artwork struck me as sub-par and some of the monster names were laughable - anyone for Jelly?  The box insert was clearly designed with another game in mind.

RATING: 7.2
An interesting, quick game, and similar in many respects to Dominion.  Ultimately, luck holds this game back from being great, as is the case (naturally) with many card games.

Update after further play:
The game starts to feel repetitive after a while.  This game felt much like Stone Age, insofar as I was initially very hooked but grew tired of the game after extended plays.

UPDATE after further further play: I have grown fonder of Thunderstone lately.  Oddly enough, this turned out to be the case with Stone Age, too.  Thunderstone, with expansions, offers a nice variety, and, for me, it's more engaging than, say, Dominion.

RATING: 9
All it took was a single 2 hour game and we knew how to play. Easy to learn for beginners and lots of fun. When it comes to games (any game not just card games) my wife and I tend to compare our enjoyment to that of Magic The Gathering. That is because it (Magic The Gathering) is an easy to learn, easy to play game that just works. This is the same thing. It just works. The mechanics are fantastic, the card art is great, the feel of the game is great and the production values are spot on. 

The game would get a 10 if it had some interaction between players, as it is right now the game feels like a 2 player game of (single player) solitaire. But it is a game where you can sit at the table, have a laugh, talk about your day, have a few drinks and relax. Well worth getting.

Edit: Score is 9 now due to solo play being just as great as when you play with other folks. Really well thought out game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
I'm not a fan of Dominion, but I can appreciate it's very elegant mechanics, and why they hold so dear to that many people. Dominion is a very well designed, elegant game with a laughably silly theme (and rather bad artwork).

Enter Thunderstone. I had great expectations about this one. The theme made sense, and the fact you're fighting monsters made for a much more compelling hype. Sadly, this all comes at the cost of the mechanics' elegance. The game is just not fun for me, and it becomes really fiddly. Plus, the extra layers make for a much more chaotic game, but with pretensions of being a gamers' game.

I might play again, but I'm not actively seeking to. That makes it a 4 (at best) in my book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
EDIT: comments below still valid, this is painfully random but more thematic dominion. but if you know what you are getting into and not expect dominion-like game too much it is fun all the same.

--

if you are a story-first kind of gamer this might appeal to you, it's hard not to respect what the author has done in this respect.

as a game, for me at least, this will not see any play.

- the game is a LOT more random than Dominion. imagine playing D but some cards allow you to buy victory points, and others action cards. how they split is suddenly a lot more important. not to mention the monsters - who cares you got 8gold, that province card is just not there at this time, buy an estate.

- the game has a very big snowball. you can find yourself out of the game rather quickly if you are unlucky enough not to get to the hero upgrades.

- the game is 2-3 times longer than dominion, and if you are at the wrong end of the snowball effect that means an hour of waiting for the obvious end.

so to me, not caring for the story aspect, this is a straight down worse variant of D. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial Impression: This deckbuilder tells a nice dungeon crawl story. Build a party of heroes. Equip them with torches, rations, weapons, and spells. Enter the dungeon. Go back to the town for rest, more equipment, and more/better personnel. The mechanism that stood out to me the most was the option to upgrade heroes using XPs, as well as the need for light sources in order to delve deeper into the dungeon. Add to that the fact that the slain monsters will cycle gold into your hand frequently makes this a deckbuilder with theme. The only detraction from theme for me was slaying many monsters increases the bloat of your deck and actually makes it more difficult to continue slaying monsters. Overall, the game was enjoyable but still suffered from what many early deckbuilders did, the feeling of multiplayer solitaire.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
If you like Dominion give this a try, if you don't give it a try. The two are similar enough I believe a Dominion player could, in the least, tolerate a game in place of Dominion, and different enough someone who doesn't enjoy Dominion might like it. The downside to the game is the game can't (from my understanding) bring itself to an end like Dominion. No one is picking up victory point cards? No problem. In Thunderstone, if no one is fighting monsters the game will drag. This could be a terrible blow for someone new to deck-builders who probably doesn't yet understand refining a deck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This was a very fun game where you build your deck by buying cards to put in it. You want to get vp's by defeating monsters. Sounds familiar? Indeed it's just Dominion with a Fantasy sauce poured over it. Admittedly there are some differences. In Thunderstone you basically have two currencies instead of one, you can level up characters (cards) by using xp and there is a fun light mechanic going on. But it still feels just like Dominion. It seems to me like this game was designed to capitalize on the succes of Dominion. Not a very gentleman way to behave. Still, if you like Dominion and you like fantasy themed games, you'll love Thunderstone.

2 rating points deducted for this less than original game.

RATING: 8
The game has a lot of little rules, but they all make sense in respect to the theme.

Played a 5 player game.  I think that made the game a bit cut throat.  There were some cards that ran out.  Everyone enjoyed it and said they'd play it again.

Played a 2 player game.  The game seemed to go on forever.  I'll have to variant this by placing the Thunderstone higher within the dungeon deck.

Played a 4 player game.  This maybe the sweet spot.  It did seem necessary to have some village card that allowed 'destroy'.  It seemed to keep the decks within a manageable level.

RATING: 3
After two plays, I can see many of the criticisms of this game. Yes, the dungeon can easily throw up monsters nobody can kill, and this can seriously slow the game down. The rules are also completely lacking when it comes to the difficult job of explaining card effect interactions. Do monsters kill heroes after the attack strength is worked out, or before it? Can you kill a hero borrowed by the level 3 magician's power? The rules are infuriatingly unclear.

I also found it particularly susceptible to AP as players struggled with different battle modifiers (addition and multiplication) for two different types of attack (basic and magic) and light. I would advise against four or five players, there's just too much downtime.

Ultimately, while I really like the central mechanic, the game seems to be a pile of mechanics that just don't work well together, causing a game of many fits and starts. Our group suggests the following houserules to make the game more fluid:

- PROGRESSIVE DUNGEON DECK - the game needs this like nothing else.

- allow immediate spend of XP gained in a battle, after that battle on heroes who took part in the battle, augmented by collected XP if needed.

- Disallow the spending of money on Lvl 2 and Lvl 3 heroes - they can only be purchased through XP.

- Instead of destroying heroes after upgrading, replace them on top of the hero deck.

RATING: 10
Great Game. Addictive. I keep playing it over and over.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I had a really long review for this game because it fell into that extremely frustrating niche of a great idea hampered by execution.

I think the wholesale appropriation of Dominion mechanics really works for the theme.  But the pacing of the game is completely mangled.  The first part of the game you build your deck and the second you mow down monsters.  There should be more of a choice between the options, but killing monsters is almost always a bad idea early.  This error is particularly egregious because neither killing monsters or buying stuff is particularly engaging or interesting.  Playing your hand in Dominion could become rote once you were experienced, but it certainly has more depth than in Thunderstone.

Secondly, the monsters should probably have been tiered.  The entire theme falls apart when you fight pushovers late game and it causes problems with worthless monsters clogging up the dungeon.

I think the coop variant actually works better since it has a time pressure that forces you to kill stuff in the dungeon while building your deck (i.e. make interesting decisions).

In short it's just kind of tedious.  Dominion had the good sense to be quite short and to end before decks become too cluttered (assuming proper play). In contrast, Thunderstone has a long repetitive buildup where you buy the best card followed by a stomping the strongest monster you can.  But the latter section can bog down since the vanquished monsters enter your deck and the number of monsters is much higher than the number of VP cards in Dominion.

Finally, the card balance is just terrible.  The wizard hero is leagues better than the others.  Fireball is crazy.  Food seems uniformly worthless.  Nothing really combos into each other in an interesting way.  You literally just buy the best cards and kill the strongest monster you can.  It's completely trivial on a decision-making level.  As is getting rid of this game.

RATING: 6
There are many aspects of this game that bother me.

First of all the starting deck: The Militias are so bad that the best strategy is to get rid of them as soon as possible. Not only is this unthematic (getting rid of all your soldiers, instead of training them) and unintuitive (new player will usually not think of this strategy) in most setups also leads to a boring start where everybody rests for the first few turns. For 3 XP upgrading a Militia isn't worth it. I think the game would be much better if it would only cost 2 XP or even 1 XP. It would make upgrading Militias an attractive option, which would be thematic and more interesting to play. 

Then there is the two different currencies (money for buying and strength for defeating monsters) and the dungeon halls: this makes this game very luck-dependent. In every deckbuilder you will have sometimes good hands and sometimes bad hands, but over the course of the game it will more or less even out. If you have a particularly bad hand in Dominion, the next one will probably be very good. But in Thunderstone timing is crucial: You need your strong hand with heroes when the the big monster with lots of victory points enters the dungeon hall. You also need the right timing to grab the level 3 heroes before the other players do.

Finally there are many cards that are just awful. You will never buy them because you don't want to have them in your deck (e.g. Battle Fury). On the other hand there are cards that are so powerful that the only viable strategy is to buy them (e.g. Trainer). And then there are cards so powerful that they are no fun to play against (Selurin, not only because of his attack multiplier, but his level 3 ability, which allows him to borrow a hero from the opponent and with the right monster even destroy it.)

For me the game was not very enjoyable until I found out that you have to get rid of your Militias and which cards are the good cards, because without this knowledge you will have very many bad hands in a pretty long and therefore frustrating game. With a better understanding of the game and playing asynchronous online on Yucata it can actually be quite fun though.

[microbadge=13167][microbadge=40] Yucata

RATING: 8
The ultimate downgrade in the rating here (from a 10 to an 8) was the eventual discovery of too many power-combos. Feast with a lot of high-power cards (Outlander, Fireball) is pretty broken, Trainer is way overpowered. A lot of basic game layouts can be busted wide open by simply playing a) Buy a Feast or Short Sword if you can, otherwise rest a Militia, then b) run high-powered heroes or fireballs. The expansions tamp this back, but nonetheless there are too many cards in the base set that we ultimately had to ban. Dragonspire + Thornwood Siege + Heart of Doom is the sweet spot for "classic" Thunderstone, and you should skip right to those sets rather than playing the original at this point. Definitely skip the first expansion.
---
Can I just make a comment? Those folks who think that Thunderstone is worse usability-wise than Dominion are smoking crack. Yes, Thunderstone could be better - one problem that people always struggle with is XP vs. VP. Yes, the icons are small, which is not awesome. But Dominion is such a flaming car wreck, with its 4-point text, indistinguishable treasures and victory cards. At least the text on the vast majority of Thunderstone cards is large enough to be legible.
---
After much play (thanks to a copy acquired at BGG.con), Thunderstone has got to be a 10. It's Dominion with nuance. The extra tactical and strategic details give it a significant edge in gameplay over Dominion, as well as the obvious advantage of a hugely successful theme. Also very importantly, Thunderstone has done away with a lot of Dominion's fluff: once you've got a hand, it plays out pretty quickly, none of this endless draw/search/action engine fiddly stuff just to see if you can get up to 8 gold for a province. There are many more interesting choices about how to build and maintain your deck, but once you've made those choices, playing the cards you draw is easy with a minimum of hassle once you get the hang of it. This, to me, is how it should be. It's too bad many gamers will be put off by the fact that it appears to be a Dominion knock-off; yes, it's a related game (OK, closely related), but it's not a knock-off.

The interesting thing is that Thunderstone has rejuvenated my interest in Dominion, which had taken a severe hammering from the Pirate Ship in Seaside. Dominion *is* shorter and cleaner. But I prefer the greater subtlety of Thunderstone.

The box says Thunderstone goes up to 5, but I'd recommend limiting it to 4. It does work with 5, but I think it's better with fewer. If you do play with 5, one option is to include an additional set of monsters (so include 4 varieties instead of 3). It does make the game longer, but at least you'll need more than 15 points to win.
---
First impressions are extremely positive. Yes, the game has heavily looted Dominion for ideas. Yes, the amount of "borrowing" here skirts the boundaries of good taste (a nod to Dominion in the credits would have been a good idea). But CCGs have been around a while, I think it successfully stays on the right side of that boundary and Thunderstone adds to and expands the space, mainly by adding a living, breathing theme, something which Dominion not only so spectacularly lacked, but which it seemed to revel in its total paste-on job.

Anyway, Thunderstone was a lot of fun, I think it has way more expansion potential than Dominion without losing thematic coherence, and if it can hold up, has the potential to replace Dominion for me. Surprised by the "too many turns with nothing to do" comments. You can always do something, thinning your deck by throwing a card away. In my 4 or 5 games I found that there were very few wasted turns, certainly not much different in this respect from Dominion.

Yes, the graphic design is not great. But is it worse than Dominion's? Dominion is such a graphic design train wreck, it's painful to see it not more substantially improved upon (Thunderstone is, in my opinion, a non-trivial improvement. But that doesn't mean it rises to the level of actually being good). Both games are stuck in the early-90s-era CCG land, back when CCG graphic designers/layout people really had no idea what they were doing, and the state of the art has moved very far on. This is not rocket science. Make important numbers larger, make icons distinct. Even I know that.

The one downside I'll perceive is that there just doesn't appear to be quite as much variety in the box as there is in the Dominion base set. The variety of monsters is great, but the heroes doesn't seem to have a ton of range, and the weapons are pretty similar. One of Dominion's strengths (at least in the base set) is its impressive range, although at the cost of about 10-15% of games being uninteresting. We'll see how Thunderstone does.

RATING: 6
I only played this once and it definitely requires a few more plays before I can finally say that I don’t like it – but this was, unfortunately, my first-hand impression. I am disappointed as I really wanted to like it and perhaps replace Dominion (I don’t play a lot of deck-building games), but after my first, and only, play with 2 players, I’m not convinced.

Normally I don’t care about the theme, but I think the theme of Thunderstone is better than the theme of Dominion (which is virtually non-existing). The concept about killing monsters in the dungeon sounded awesome. However, even though the theme is better, I think Dominion is better in almost every other way. You thought there was a small amount of interaction in Dominion? Well, try Thunderstone! In the basic setup there is only a single card which affects your opponents… Perhaps this has changed in the expansions, or perhaps other cards in the base set allows for more interaction – I certainly hope so as it felt almost like playing solitaire! Granted, we were only 2, but this wouldn’t change with more players – all it would do was for the game to last longer – something I definitely don’t think this game needs! I think it takes too long considering the enjoyment I get from this deck-building game compared to other games in the genre. 

Further complaints of mine are that you don’t really want to attack in the beginning in order to avoid clogging your deck with poor cards (though some monsters have an ability you can use). I think you need to spend too much time in the village or simply resting in the beginning, something I wasn’t fond of. Regarding the monsters, if you draw some strong monsters in the beginning, you might have a hard time get going (and nobody wants to remove it so the other players can attack the other monsters more easily). I did like the special abilities and how different the monsters were, though. Finally, I didn’t understand why you have so much gold available each turn, but the cards cost so little – and you have only one buy?! It felt very weird to have 8-9 gold in your hand and a single card when almost every card had a cost of 3-4-5 gold…

One thing I really liked about the game, at least in theory, is the level-up mechanism and the experience points! To me, this is the only reason why I would like to play Thunderstone again. Personally I had too much xp in the end of the game as it was more beneficial to attack instead of upgrading your cards; but I hadn’t noticed that the level 3 heroes grant you 2 VPs, so I guess that would have changed my decisions. The xp mechanic made the game feel like the computer game Diablo or something like that, at least on surface; you have crappy gear, you slay monsters, you get xp, you buy better stuff and is therefore able to kill bigger monsters.

RATING: 5
This game has me really torn.  I want to like it - there's a definite improvement on the basic formula of Dominion here.  But after over a dozen plays I just don't think it's much fun.

The twist on the average deck-building game is you're not just buying up cards, but also trying defeat monsters in the dungeon.  Each has certain requirements to be defeated, so you need to get the right combo of cards to come up in your hand.  That random element can be enormously frustrating, which at times makes me wonder why I'm not just rolling dice.

I'm not writing this game off by any means, but I don't think I need to own it now.  Wouldn't turn down the offer to play a game - it's just not my favorite.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Components: Jason Engle's art is exceptional. Every single card is a joy to look at. Excellent quality cards.

The relevant numbers (cost, gold, XP, VP) need to be larger, as they are referenced constantly; I'd also appreciate if the gold value were in the top left corner, and the attack/magic value were in the top right corner, just for easier indexing. Or perhaps the hero level could be in the top left corner. Or the hero/village/monster symbol in the top left corner. Put something in the top left corner!

There is a ton of tiny text on all the cards. It's a hassle to try to arrange the village cards so that even two people can read them without getting neck strain. And it's nearly impossible to read my opponent's upside down cards from across the table, which makes it very difficult to audit each other (and with all the special powers interacting, this game requires a lot of auditing, because we are constantly accidentally breaking rules).

I want the higher level heroes to have more pimped out images, so they look tougher and more rugged. A level 3 hero should look like it could squash a level 1 hero. Also, I wish the heroes had more traditional (or at least more pronounceable) races. Elf and Dwarf are fine; Selurin, Thyrian, and Faeyn are tough to pronounce, and don't really correlate to any fantasy race in my mind. All the made up names start to blur together.

Several other component problems are fixed by later expansions: plastic XP chips; indexable card dividers; a better storage box. The attempt to distinguish the randomizer cards from the regular cards, and from each other, with different card backs would have been more successful if the backings were more different; a slightly different color and slightly different border isn't good enough.

Even the updated rules (1.4) are a bit of a mess. Huge paragraphs obscure important rules and nuances. The glossary fails to address at least half of our card-related questions. We have questions about very basic things (if I destroy my weapon to gain its bonus, do I still get its base value, and is it still equipped or can I equip another weapon?). [Edit 2013-10-22. Most rule questions have been resolved by the latest version of the rules, in Numenera.]

Sometimes there is a disconnect between the monster name/image/health. Something called “The Unchained” that looks like the the most badass demon dragon I've ever seen should have more than 5 health.

Experience: Recruiting heroes, weapons, and spells into my party, then sending a small band into a dungeon is just plain fun. Fun fun fun. Stream-lining my deck is very important (and there are several clever ways to get rid of cards). Also, there are often a few different permutations of heroes + equipment to consider before deciding which monster to face.

The concept of light is excellent. It's highly thematic, finally bringing into play something that always seemed somewhat immaterial in D&D. Depending on how readily available light is, it can become a huge factor in my deck.

Most of my favorite heroes from the series come from the base set; right out of the box there is a lot of variety, and many interesting permutations of cards. I appreciate the monster groups, such that each dungeon will have a certain thematic and functional feel to it, from the intersection of the three groups, but that each group contains several unique monsters. The theme is everything here.

(It's so much cooler to buy a Fireball spell in Thunderstone that any card I can think of in Dominion. I was thinking about Dominion the other day, and thought ironically that Dominion is like “Thunderstone: The Card Game”, in that it's much simpler, has way less theme, plays faster, and isn't as much fun. Without Dominion we probably wouldn't have Thunderstone, but now that I'm playing Thunderstone, I no longer desire to play Dominion.)

There are times when I have a sweet hand, and must choose between venturing into the dungeon, or visiting the village to buy a juicy card and/or level some heroes. Even when it is obvious whether to go to the village or dungeon, it's usually still interesting to decide exactly what to do when I get there.

The concept of the heroes is really cool. They feel distinct, and I love leveling them up. I really like that we must equip weapons to heroes before we can use those weapons; and that the heroes must be strong enough to carry the weapons. The five different classes of heroes (fighter, wizard, cleric, thief, archer) all feel very different, and each serve their purpose in my deck. It seems a bit strange that we can cast spells without magic users in the party (I don't like to think of myself as part of my party, venturing into the deep). Might be a better thematic choice if a cleric or wizard had to be present to cast a spell.

Each random assortment of monsters/heroes/village cards brings with it is own challenges to be overcome. A puzzle to be solved, to figure out how to get light, to discard militia, to handle disease, etc. It's a joy to find the synergy between the cards available, and the best way to counter the monsters. More cards means more possibilities. I've been really impressed with how the game rewards diversity. Putting together a deck that balances gold, light, magic/attack, strength, weapons, spells, etc., is super fun. That being said, it typically doesn't pay to have too many different strategies going on at once.

Runs a bit long (averaging 90 minutes for 2-player games), but cool, cool, cool. And actually, we're always a bit disappointed when the thunderstone is finally revealed, because it's always just as our decks are getting humming. I can easily play this several times in one sitting, and am constantly wanting to play it.

We played three games with the 'epic' set up, where all cards are available, but sorted into random piles by type (weapon x3, spell x2, villager x2, items; archers, fighters, clerics, thieves, wizards). It actually meant a longer set-up and break down time, which I wasn't expecting. And wasn't as much fun. There were the same number of options on a given turn, but because the options kept changing from turn to turn, we had to spend a lot of time reading all the cards. And with only a slim chance of getting the same hero multiple times, it was hard to get pure synergy going in the decks. The lack of competition for certain heroes was missed, and we ended up with all sorts of gold and no level 2+ heroes to spend them on. We're going back to playing the basic setup. We've also tried with having some selection method from the randomizers (e.g. draw 8 heroes, keep 4), but are once again delighting in the challenge of a purely random village.

With two players, the competition for heroes is very stiff. With three players, it's actually a bit less stiff because the game is so much shorter that it makes more sense to just go to the dungeon for any monsters I can. I can't imagine what it's like in a four-player game. Must be brutal. I wonder if higher level heroes are purchased (rather than leveled to) more often than not.

RATING: 6
While better than Race for the Galaxy, not nearly as fluid or fun as Dominion.  This is a deck building game with a twist, instead of buying victory points, you usually kill creatures to earn them.

The major problem with this game is that it inherently is driven by LUCK than by player skill.  Dominion is a game that is based upon players building up their "expected value" or "average" when buying $ cards.  Once you can expect to get a certain amount of $ per hand, you can then start buying victory cards for the win.  How you go about raising up your average $/hand is your strategy.  Players choices are thus extremely important, but luck plays a part in this.  Usually, player choices are far more important than just dumb luck.

That kind of game play is impossible with Thunderstone.  In thunderstone you still build up your "average" $/card, but it can never be high enough to buy the cards you want consistently and in a reasonable amount of time.  In addition, even if you did build up enough $ to buy a certain number of combat cards, you then can NEVER build up enough combat cards to consistently defeat the creatures in the game, on average.

So since you can't push your averages over 50%, you depend quite heavily on the variance of your deck, and thus you luck.  Thus it's not a game of skill and maneuver, but a game of LUCK!  Player choices are negated and victory is squarely in the hands of chance. Why even play the game?  

And this game also suffers from the "card counting" problem of all card based games.  If you lose track of what you have, good luck competing in this game.

So overall while better than Race, it pales in comparison to Dominion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
We'll assume everyone can tell this is a Dominion ripoff and move on.

Horrible graphic design.  There are lots of numbers on the card but no symbology to remind you what the number means.  They are also so small you can't see what your opponents are doing and have to pretty much take it on faith that their plays are legal.

As far as mechanics, the basics are similar to Dominion but the additions are clunky.  The "light/dark" mechanic is overly unwieldy and confusing.  Just a straight +/- attack value would have been better.  The mechanic of having the monsters get more "into the light" is thematic but they needed to have a little board to put the cards on with the cheat-sheet of what the attack affect would be and not bother distinguishing light/dark vs. attack.

Strength vs. attack ended up being confusing too.  More than one person accidentally applied "hero strength" to attack instead of to what they could carry. 

Weapon carrying rules (as explained, I didn't read them) didn't make sense - unless a hero has more than two hands they should not be able to use more than two weapons regardless of strength... maybe this was explained incorrectly though.  

Summary - it was ok but not an improvement to its inspiration and I have a bad taste about just how much was ripped off from Dominion w/o a credit.

(Not same as San Juan vs. RftG -- both of those games were specifically designed as a Puerto Rico card game by different designers and San Juan was the chosen one, so RftG got a new theme -- it is much more acceptable that the two games are mechanically similar with that in mind.)

RATING: 8
Dominion with a fantasy theme, except that it replaces racing for gold with killing monsters and levelling up.

In my opinion this makes for a more interesting game than Dominion, not only because of the theme, but because there's more importance on interaction between cards within each hand. This, in turn, leads to greater variability in the strategies and choices each player makes. And that, to me, is what makes a game good.

There are some complaints people have with the possibility of the starting monsters being too difficult, and the inability to wean your deck of monster cards without losing their VP. The good news is that there are some simple variants out there that address these issues, and I would encourage your local group to use them if you're getting annoyed.

Anyway, thumbs up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
[2012-05-05 Rating: n.a. -> 5]
After playing this game online at yucata many times and one face to face game last night, I finally came up with a rating for this one. As it stands, I don't really care for Thunderstone. When I first heard about the game, I thought that it was based on a nice idea. So I was really looking forward to my first few games. Guess what, my anticipation was replaced by disappointment. And here is why:

* Nearly each game of Thunderstone I played dragged on for what felt like days. While Dominion feels like a light-hearted race game, Thunderstone moves along at an awful slow pace. Sure, there where some games that had the right mix of cards and therefore were fun to play, but Thunderstone couldn't deliver this feeling constantly. I feel like the game could have needed some more devolpment time and some tweaking of the card mix and abilities. While in Dominion nearly every mix of cards plays well, it's the other way around in Thunderstone. So if you find a good mix, stay with it.

* The thing that should be the most fun, namely fighting monsters in the dungeon, is way to mathy for my liking. Don't get me wrong, as a Knizia fan I do like some numbers in my game, but the calculation of attack value is just to involved for my liking. It sucks the fun out of the game for me if I have to watch people calculating all the time.

* Somehow I have the feeling that luck plays a too great role in Thunderstone. While I'm sometimes stuck with a bad draw in Dominion, this seems to happen more consistently in Thunderstone. This kills a lot of the fun me.

RATING: 7
update 2016-02-27

Thunderstone has taken a backseat on my shelf for about a year. Recently, I broke it out to test it as a solo game. Lo and behold: It works. It even works fine. Maybe on my n-th attempt I'm finally getting the hang of the light rules. Maybe I'm finally getting around to not having to look up all the iconography in the dungeon just to see whether my fighter can carry that short sword ;) -- Anyway, as a solo game, Thunderstone is pretty good, and *maybe* in a year or so, I can bring it to the table in my game group, fully confident of all the little rules .

----

somehow, I never got the hang of this. I like Dominion well enough, I really like Star Realms, so deckbuilders are fine with me. But in this one, between the Light Rules (did I mention I HATE them?) and the downtime, I don't know... it just never felt thematic enough to justify all those calculations. And I hate when you get a mixture of wounds and killed monsters as your hand and can't do much but buy something cheap in the village.

Plus, I never quite got why it wasn't co-op: To me, it hardly makes sense to see those monster get out of the dungeon and possibly attack the village, but at the same time bickering about who gets to kill them.

RATING: 6
A poor man's version of Dominion (in my view). Even less interaction, too much waiting for people to count up if they can defeat a monster or not and not enough interesting choices when buying cards.

It really needs an expansion or two that adds more interaction to the game.

I do think it will and can become much better with repeated play, as it's less fiddly than Dominion at least, but not sure it will replace Dominion as my game of choice in this category.

Update: The game is growing on me so close to a 8 now. Must be the old RPG-er in me. Can't wait for the expansions.

Update 2: I really, really want to like this game more than I do, but simply can't. At first it thought the expansion fixed some of the issues I had with the game but now I'm not sure any more. With the added traps and more dangerous breach effect it's more and more a super random take that game than a game of deck-building and planning. 

"Oh, you got hit with that trap and lost your best level 3 hero and two of your best weapons while I lost a Dagger, oh how fun this is."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players are equiping a dungeon raiding party, to beat the beasties and find the Thunderstone.  Players take turns either visiting the village to buy a card, rest to trash a card, or visit the dungeon to try and collect some victory points.  A deck drafting game very much in the same vein as Dominion, but with a bit more subtlety here.  You're no longer just racing to bang a deck together to get to the magic 8 gold, instead you're carefully constructing a dungeon party to face off many different types of monsters.  The various card effects really create a nice puzzle to be solved every turn, and the theme is brilliantly applied here... certainly improves on Dominion for me.    Caveat, play at least 2 or 3 games before passing judgment - it takes that long to get use to the icons and flow of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Gosh, how to critique this on its merits? The blatant appropriations from Dominion aside, this is a poor game. But setting it beside its inspiration further illuminates its flaws. 

The game is a card-based game, yet it doesn't fully utilize obvious things that could be done with cards: discard, card flow, extra draw, interaction with opponents' hands, etc. 

Thunderstone is largely based on 'buy cards according to obvious strategy', and then hoping your draws are lucky enough to overcome the deck of challenges. 

Few in-game decisions seem to matter after you've gone down an initial path. Perhaps later expansions might add some game-play options. But with Dominion or a real CCG as alternatives, why bother?

RATING: 4
11/25/2009 - The game has a Dominion type of engine where players are building their own decks, which is definitely a popular mechanic.

So popular in fact, that I think people initially like this game due to the mechanic.  Overall, the game lacks in the significantly interesting decisions that Dominion has.  This was my decision tree:

#1- Can I beat monsters in the dungeon?  If yes, then kill the one worth the most VPs

#2 - If #1=NO what can I afford in the village.  Proceed to buy the best card

#3 - Repeat.

Which did not lead to interesting choices for me.  Just some optimization of obvious values.  I will give it one more shot before it is banished forever.  Rating = 5

--------------------------------------------------

12/05/09 - Further playing with speedier players and a setup other than the suggested starting proved to be much more interesting.  The key is to be playing almost simultaneously since there is so much information to track in this game.

The faster it went, the better.  Plus more interesting decisions opened up.  Although it did feel like much of the decisions were at the beginning of the game on strategy and it was just a matter of execution.

Rating upgrade to 7

-----------------------------

12/08/2009 - The game has solitaire rules available online.  Too bad it was not in the rulebook, still gives the game a slight upgrade.  Although the novelty is starting to wear thin on me.

Rating upgrade to 7.3

RATING: 7
I was worried this might be too much of a Dominion clone, but they don't really feel the same to me, despite borrowing mechanics.   Dominion is more about deck building, efficiency, speed, pruning, finesse, etc.  Thunderstone is about gathering heros, weapons, and smashing monsters in the face with flaming swords.  The artwork is fantastic and consistently so, since it was all done by one person.  The card frames, however, and icons could be better executed.  The game play is a little slower than Dominion, but that didn't bother me since there is a lot to consider.   Wouldn't want to play with 5 players, 3 seems nice.  Expansions should serve this game well.   In general, I think I would choose this over Dominion most of the time.

RATING: N/A
Extremely underdeveloped, to the point where some games may be degenerate due to, e.g., lacking magic attack but that being required to kill certain enemies. This is "solved" by allowing players to give up a turn to banish an enemy to the bottom of the deck, but this creates a dilemma - who's going to give up their own turn to benefit the other players, who will get first crack at the new enemy?

Also, the "basic" set suggested in the rules is simply wrong. It includes guys who should never be purchased as they are less useful than the others.

Otherwise, I like the overall transition from building an engine that enables purchasing to building an engine that slays monsters. It's unclear to me how much to value cards that do both decently.

RATING: N/A
A nice variation from Dominion, and also adds a purpose and goal to deck building.  Unlike Dominion (where you are trying to buy victory points), you need to build a deck of heroes and equip them with the proper items to defeat monsters in the dungeon.  You gain VP and XP from defeating monsters, but the monsters are worth little gold and will inflate your deck.  XP can be used to level up your heroes and VP wins you the game.

RATING: 7
(As of [b]4[/b] plays) A deck-building game with a more immersive theme than [GAMEID=36218], but with markedly less variety, strategy and interactivity. Some set-ups are fun, but many simply do not work well enough to justify the time. A good game should be enjoyable even when you are losing. Dominion succeeds in this. Thunderstone doesn't quite.

(As of [b]5[/b] online plays) I love playing this game online. The downtime problem is eliminated and, even though scores are updated automatically, falling far behind the leader doesn't seem quite so onerous. I'm enjoying it so much online that I'm ready to revisit it in person.

[b]1 year verdict:[/b] My opinion of this has improved due to the slickness of online play, but in person it is still a bit too clunky for it to supplant Dominion.

RATING: N/A
Dominion + MtG = Thunderstone?  The designer was involved in designing Magic the Gathering for ten years, and helped develop 30 sets, has taken some of the ideas of Dominion to make a stand alone game called Thunderstone, which incorporate even more MtG inspired ideas.  More here: http://www.boardgamenews.com/index.php/boardgamenews/comments/designer_diary_thunderstone/
Card interaction seems less than Dominion, on MtG gamer wrote: "Do you like how blue and black decks play mechanically? go with Dominion. Do you prefer Red and Green decks? Thunderstone for you."

RATING: 10
Possibly my new favorite game.  The play is similar to Dominion, with players dealing themselves six cards from their personal deck.  But that's where the similarity ends and Thunderstone leaves Dominion in the dust because Thunderstone actually has a narrative.  The players go to a village to buy objects, weapons, spells, and heroes, and then travel to a dungeon to use their heroes to fight monsters.  The experience points gained from beating the monsters can be used to level up the heroes into even tougher fighters/magicians.

The players can fight with magic or with brute force (although some monsters are immune to one or the other).  As the dungeon gets darker the further in the players go, and extra force is required to overcome the light penalties, the player's strategies require more than simply getting the biggest warrior they can with the most brute force. 

Play is fast, with some lag time as players calculate whether they can defeat a particular monster.  The artwork is great.  Cannot say enough good things about this game.

The availability of expansion packs with new heroes, monsters, and objects mean that this game will remain fresh after multiple plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Ok, after numerous plays I've come to realize that this just is not a very good game. It's clunky, scales horribly with more players, and it is just frustrating. This is the part that bothers me mostly about the game. And with only 4 hero stacks, they go quickly. Right behind that are the high dollar weapon cards. You get these early ensuring some nice high dollar hands in the near future, but there is no other source of getting money into your hand, meaning that once those weapons are gone, your deck starts diluting without any chance of repair.

RATING: 5
Dominion: The Dungeon. At least pretty much. It may not be as streamlined as Dominion but it makes it up in theme. I actually like Thunderstone better than Dominion (admittedly that's not saying much) for a couple of reasons.

First of all it feels a lot less abstract thanks to the fantasy theme. Going to the village, hiring heroes, equipping them, and then entering the dungeon to battle monsters somehow feels more satisfying than "I scraped together enough gold to buy a duchy, yeah". It's also much harder - if not impossible - to burn through your deck at the same ridiculous rate so you're not necessarily bound to see the same nauseating, sleep-inducing combo a million times in a row. There are actually reasons to diversify. Hooray.

That said, the light mechanic feels a little clunky, not being able to level up when the higher level heroes have all been hired doesn't make sense, and the end game condition is pretty arbitrary. So not all is gold that twinkles, but still, better than Dominion.

RATING: 7
The first game was much more positive than expected. Similar enough to Dominion to be comparable, yet different enough to be its own viable game in its own right. 

The strategy is a bit off for a dungeon crawl as you tend to manipulate the rules to improve your hand instead of simply killing all the bad guys. Jumping in and failing to kill a monster just so it will destroy your militia is a viable and strong strategy. It's still plenty of fun, just thematically screwy from time to time.

Downgraded to a seven. There are too many set ups that just don't work. You have to know this game inside and out to be able to see when you are going to run into a game where you just won't have adequate cards in the supply to combat the monsters.

And upgraded to an 8. Yucata.de takes all the annoying parts out of Thunderstone and allows you to play in a purely fun environment. This is the first time where I have found online play to be more fun than in person play. My only complaint is that the game only has so much life in it without expansions.

After  Facebook Thunderstone, I have downgraded the game back to a 7. It's a pretty great game, but you have to know it well to enjoy the nuances that aren't apparent. By the time you get to that point, it is hard to find anyone to play with as they have to be just as dedicated to understand the great parts in the game. 

...and then Facebook Thunderstone went away. Lame.

RATING: 6
ALSO OWN:

Thunderstone Promo Pack (2011)
Doomgate Legion
Dragonspire
Wrath of the Elements

I still kind of dig this game, but as the card pool increases, so too does the possibility of completely unworkable card pools.  With so many dependencies of cards on other certain card types, this is inevitable.  So you end up with creatures with physical immunity, but no magical weapons nor characters with magical attacks.  Game over, g'night.  Or a character who depends on a certain weapon type, but not one of them makes it into the pool.  Hey, prepare to gather cobwebs, hero dude.

Once Nightfall came out, my interest in this plummeted.

It's still got great artwork, I love the theme, and it still is like that crazy drunk uncle who shows up at parties and does batshit insane stuff that just makes you laugh.  Thunderstone, if it were a high school student, would be that one from the 70s who drove around in a van with an airbrushed picture of a bald eagle with a confederate flag headband playing electric guitar.

RATING: 5
This is a dungeon crawl with the added feature of drawing your team of adventurers and their special equipment from a deck that you will build and refine each turn - instead of a dice roll to determine if you beat the monstor or trap, it is the draw of cards to your deck that provides the random factor. The game does not use  many of the clever deck engine mechanics of Dominion, so the main game mechanic is card drafting - when you deal yourself an appropriately strong hand then enter the dungeon to slay monsters and gain VP. Very good components and art. The theme has some disconnects such as being able to upgrade your heroes with experience points earned by fighting monsters - or you can just buy a higher level hero from the draft pool! - intended to make a "money" strategy viable but readily house-ruled out to make only experience gained from detroying monters able to upgrade your heroes - as it should be! Benefits from using the card randomiser available from bgg as certain monster/village/hero card combinations can be tedious.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The art is great! I like Thunderstone's theme way more than Dominion's...but the game drags a bit.

Intelligently populating the village card piles is a MUST! Don't rely on pure randomization or this game will drag on and on as no one can kill anything and keep the game in motion.

I would suggest buying the Doomgate legion expansion as well. The treasure cards can help out a great deal in keeping the game running smoothly.

Trap cards are detrimental and can really kill a game. (Bye bye heroes...hello useless deck!)

Basically...a fast game of Thunderstone > Dominion IMO....but a SLOW game of Thunderstone < Dominion.

RATING: 9.2
i can't see the reason for its comparison to dominion. they are two very different games, asides from the deck building mechanic. i must say that i much prefer thunderstone. though coming from dominion, i'm very used to chaining my action cards to optimize my buys, and so when playing thunderstone, i was disappointed to draw a hand with lots of gold but only have one buy to spend it on. other than that, it's a very enjoyable game that's just oozing with theme. to spice the game up, i recommend incorporating some of the rules from thunderstone advance: the additional option of being able to "prepare" for your next turn, and having to fight a guardian monster for the thunderstone. it just felt kind of anti-climatic before, to get the thunderstone after defeating a low health monster in rank 1.

RATING: 7
Ah, Thunderstone.

On the one hand, Thunderstone has a very good system, one that builds on the stunning model of Dominion but does very much its own thing with it. It embraces its theme and makes great use of it.

RATING: 6
This was one of the first deck-building games that were published after Dominion and it shows its legacy.  The turns are faster in Thunderstone because you mostly use the cards you get instead of chaining a lot of actions. I also like the theme more, but there doesn’t seem to be as much depth in game play and it feels more the same each time you play it. Also, sometimes the hardest monsters come out first in the game, meaning that there isn't a real story arc and make the game stop up. People were asking for a more interactive Dominion, but this is not the game. Actually, I think there are more ways in Dominion to screw other players’ decks. That said, the game as an activity is quite fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One play with three, two with four. Fairly enjoyable. I think it does some things better than Dominion, some things worse. Obviously the theme is more coherent, and it's fun when everything comes together in your hand and you're able to take down a nasty monster. On the other hand, Dominion seems to be the more subtle and interesting game. I could be wrong, but I feel like Thunderstone will get old quickly without a steady injection of new cards.

One substantial complaint: horrible job on the card iconography, AEG. Everything's in little circles! The first time you play the game, how are you supposed to remember which corner is VP, which is XP, and which is strength? You could have branched out to other shapes, perhaps, like maybe you could have put the numbers in little chubby exes or vees. I'm not trying to tell you how to run your company or anything, but the funny thing about circles is that one looks just like another.

One more thing: if you're going to rip off Dungeons & Dragons, why not at least have it make sense? I mean, why the hell is there a flying horse in a dungeon? Did it get lost? Did it lose a bet? And a griffin, too! Why not an entire airplane? Let's have the whole frigging air force down there underground where they'll be as useful as a hand crank on a pith helmet.

RATING: 7
I have mixed feelings about this game. There isn't much to the gameplay. It is very random and repetitive, while choices are usually limited and easy. The unclear rules, constantly updated by the publisher, are annoying. So is the constant shuffling. It borrows too heavily from "Dominion", a game I don't like. There are also graphic design issues, with nearly indistinguishable icons and small font. Despite all that, I have enjoyed the game and want to play more. But I realize my enjoyment of it has much to do with the Fantasy theme and excellent artwork. I'm just afraid it will not take long until the novelty wears off, and then I may be much less willing to play it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So it's Dominion, and yet it isn't. Its qualities are directly the inverse of Dominion's--it's got good theming, you'll never focus on just 1 or 2 cards (or money), and you'll never have endless "+draw +action" turns (that usually end up with 3 money anyway).
On the other hand, the iconography and game text is a borderline nightmare and the game takes a bit too long. The random monster setup can lead to strange situations, and the balance is off in places. 
Overall, I prefer this to Dominion, but I do feel like it could have kept more of Dominion's strengths while improving on its weaknesses. I am very much looking forward to the expansion.
[EDIT] Simple variant that makes things all kinds of better--show five monsters (or even four) instead of three in the dungeon hall. 
[EDIT] Use the Intelligent Randomizer online. It's not a neccesity, but man does it improve things.

RATING: 7
If base Dominion is the minimalist reduction of deck-building for deck-building's sake, then Thunderstone is it's younger, slightly bloated cousin, full of flavor, but slower moving, with a greater palette of colors and probably a wider range of strategies, but not as focused, and certainly not as rushed.

Thunderstone added a story to Dominion's abstraction, and added a serious helping of Fantasy RPG to the Mediaeval backdrop, while removing the slightly Political overtones.

Thieves, Brawlers, Villagers of all stripes, yes, they share these, but Thunderstone is a story, Dominion is a cat fight.

Thunderstone is a trip through a Dungeon deck (the timer) heading for the possible recovery of a magical relic, the eponymous Thunderstone.  A random set of monsters makes up that deck, and players use a set of Village cards (resources) and Hero cards (combat/resources) to venture through the Dungeon, heading for the bottom of the stack.

Thunderstone tends to start slowly, similar to Dominion, but I find it builds more slowly, and deck-thinning may be slightly less important here (I do tend to play solo, which probably influences that judgment).

Often the pace of the game is dictated by the match of Enemies in the Dungeon deck to the Heroes; sometimes the Heroes match up well and even an early player deck will defeat low and mid-ranged Enemies, sometimes it does not match as well and Warriors need unavailable magical assistance, particular classes are removed, or Sorcerers and Clerics face tough creatures they cannot easily hurt.

This is not unlike Dominion: some sets of cards are simply more fun to play than others.

I rate both Dominion and Thunderstone a 7; I like them both for different things, elegance/ease of play vs. story/struggle.

Both sets are expanded by many sets of cards and some added rules as they go along. For Thunderstone I have not found that this changes the game much, only adding to the card selection; for Dominion the card selection is the game, and the sets make perhaps a more profound change in the experience.

I like them both, and I'm glad I picked this up- it adds a little Fantasy RPG feeling back into my life that has been missing since D&D.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Wow! I really dig this game! I'd held off buying it for a long time; on paper it looks like it's going to be a straight Dominion rehash, and since I've got and enjoy Dominion, what would be the point of a rehash?

But after several plays, I can tell ya that it sure don't *feel* like Dominion: this is an Ameritrash dungeon crawl in Dominion clothing! You don't have the same ability to control your deck or form a "machine" as you do in Dominion, it's much more about taking the hand you've got and doing the best you can with it.

If I've got a complaint, it's that I wish the dungeon aspect were less deterministic as a rule. You generally always know if you've got the firepower to go into the dungeon, so it can be kinda rote. But for those heroes with a "draw card" power, it gets really interesting, as you're more inclined to take a risk and try for a monster that you might not take down with a bad draw.

But that aside, I look forward to playing this more! Like Dominion, the game will continue to show itself in new light with different random card arrays.

**UPDATE**
OK, I've played this about a dozen times, now, and the bloom is off of the rose. The dungeon aspect is really silly and boring. It doesn't at all feel like you're questing: you're buying monsters with attack points. I don't need to play this again any time soon.

**UPDATE**
Sold to a friend who liked it more. A fast life cycle from new and exciting to lame and weak for me.

RATING: 4
Imagine Dominion with no action cards, just money and points, but with different currencies available for getting money and points.  If you think this sounds tedious, well, I agree.  And that's pretty much exactly what Thunderstone is. The cards' powers don't let you manipulate your deck or draw cards or do other things of substance. They are just more or less powerful for getting the monsters (victory points) or for getting other cards that allow you to do so. (3 plays)

Earlier comments: I'm not sure who decided that Dominion needed a theme, but I certainly don't think that the theme is worth the cost in the smooth gameplay that is Dominion's greatest asset. The problems are probably just a function of the way the theme is implemented.  It's clunky and counter-intuitive, with lots of adding and the potential for things to very easily get blocked.  No need to play again.

RATING: 9
邦題：　サンダーストーン
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.25
Sept. 6, 2010: After having experienced Dominion I came into this expecting a lot being as it's mechanic core is the same. Improvements? Not so much. When you play this game, instead, you find yourself much more frustrated due to the fact that the cards don't interact with each other very well at all. There is a major balance issue. Every card is either made to be a good village card or a good fighting card. Therefore if you find yourself with a 50/50 mix in your hand you're screwed with either choice you make. The town guard is a great example of this.

The light mechanic that they implemented needs a lot more refinement. There were times when I had nothing but light in my hand but it didn't do me a bit of good because it doesn't infer any bonus, just negative effects when lacking but in order to ensure you draw some in your hand you load the deck with it. Well, during the first game but after you figure out that it's better to buy a weapon to just add 2+ to your overall attack damage, light is no longer an issue. Speaking of which, another card interaction that doesn't work is items and how they have to be equiped on characters. Numerous times in the game I drew items into my hand that couldn't be utilized due to weaker characters.

On the upside, the theme to this game was far superior to Dominion. The idea of leading a band of heroes against the monsters in a dungeon was done well. I can see how they tried to execute this with the mechanics that they put in place but it really should have been done with a lot more finesse. The aspect that really works are the illustrations. I love 'em! The monsters are all wonderful as well as the heroes and the items. Each brings out the geek in me and makes me want to play.

All and all I can't say this game is absolute trash but it is by far nothing great. It is very much a 5 for me. Take it or leave it and I have a feeling that with more plays it may go the way of munchkin and get old very quickly.

RATING: 6
Egads, it actually works!

First off, I couldn't care less about the particular theme of Thunderstone. Games don't get extra points from me for being dungeon-crawls. That being said, Thunderstone is a fine game, even if one just considers the mechanics. And the theme does work well here.

It's longer than Dominion, and more complex, the latter both in terms of the amount of rules, and the complexity of the decisions you have to make during the game. The rules are less clean than those of Dominion, and the graphical layout is horrible.

Having played three games, my first impression is that it is a more interesting game than Dominion, but also longer and more fiddly. Better or worse? I don't know yet.

I do look forward to trying Thunderstone again, to see if it can maintain its initial very favorable impression.

RATING: 6
2012-10-30

Initial Rating : 6.5 (February 2012)

Warning: I love Dominion

I wish Dominion had this theme, that is the ONLY advantage Thunderstone has over Dominion. Having saying that, I enjoy Thunderstone from time to time. It is an ok game and my second favorite deck builder (or fourth if you count the hybrids Mage Knight and A Few Acres of Snow). It is more similar to Resident Evil than to Dominion, although I like that the monsters are visible BEFORE you go to the dungeon, but they add another luck factor to the game. (In resident Evil you don’t know what you are facing so it has even more luck).  If you can defeat the monster early on, the game can take a long time to play and this happens a lot.

The art is decent, better than in Dominion but in general the graphic design can be a little confusing. (This doesn’t not happen in Dominion)

Thunderstone is more complex than Dominion, but that added complexity doesn’t translate to fun. For example I don’t like that in Thunderstone you have 3 currencies (Money, Light, Attack power) and balancing them is not as fun as I thought. What I’m saying is that attacking the monsters in the dungeon is not as fun as it should be. I don’t feel I’m “destroying” them so the theme starts to crack. Also the experience counters come to mind as unnecessary complexity. 

In my initial games I always drew 5 cards instead of 6 because I’m used to play Dominion a lot :S.

I have mostly played online so I have had no access to all the cards, but from what I have seen, I’m a little worried about balance issues, there are some cards and combos that appear to be very powerful, although as every player has access to them it is not a big deal.
 
In conclusion, I will play again this Dominion clone from time to time, but Dominion is still king for me. I would like to try some expansions which many people claim make the game better. 

Current Rating: 6.0
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
To say Thunderstone is "better than Dominion" would be overly subjective, and I'm not sure it would even be accurate.

Thunderstone seems to deliver what I was looking for, and didn't find, in Dominion.  It's longer, which gives it more time to develop a story arc.  There are two different things to do (and resources to manage):  Gold/Village turns, and Attack/Dungeon turns.  I suppose it's still an economic engine game at the core, but Thunderstone applies a veneer of top-down Ameritrashy theme that is very welcome in this burgeoning genre of "buy cards, reshuffle, buy more cards" games.

Rough around the edges as it may be, no one will accuse Thunderstone of being soulless.  Unless perhaps one's fighting the undead.

RATING: 8
I can't understand any argument that states this is very different than Dominion.  Gameplay is simple - build an efficient deck of the available cards to earn points.  Thunderstone gives you an option on your turn - buy a card OR kill a monster.  It's rarely an option - either you can kill a monster OR you can't, so you buy a card.

With all that, I like the game quite a bit.  But I'm also a big Dominion fan so that shouldn't be a surprise.  I certainly don't believe it's different enough to warrant owning both.  It's a great alternative for someone who didn't get into Dominion.

RATING: 8
A great deck building game filled with a fantasy theme. Plays super well solo or with opponents. Plenty of great cards coming out all the time with the expansions. Check out on how to play the epic game. I prefer this method. You get to play with all cards in the village. Choose monster's level based on their victory points. All heroes are available in four separte piles. Just the level one heroes, still have to upgrade to get the higher levels. Great art workd. A little time consuming to set up when doing a basic set up. You have to choose random heroes, monsters and village cards. Find them in the box and set up. Clean up takes time because all the cards are pretty well mixed up. In epic, once they cards are split into the right categories, set up is slightly quicker. Clean up is also speeded up. 
Games can go a little long, as everyone is building up a good group of heroes and weapons and support cards. Well worth buying and you will be pulling this off of your shelf for friends and playing the super solo game. Enjoy.

RATING: 8
UPDATE:
=======
I've gotten over my disappointment in this not being more of an adventure game than it is, and I've played it quite a bit now.  I'm happy to say that I think this is a fantastic game.  I haven't played any of the Dominion expansions.  So, I won't compare, but Thunderstone has a lot more going on than vanilla Dominion.  Good stuff.

Original Comments:
==================
This has -just- enough of its own thing going on to call it more than a Dominion re-theme.  It's a fun, but personally disappointing game as I had hoped for a two-layered deck building system - one where you would have a very small (1-6 cards) hero deck comprising the adventuring party which would be persistent once played, and then a larger deck for hero items, actions, and special abilities.  Perhaps the heroes could be injured fighting monsters and have to be healed by a cleric, find a potion, or return to town.  Perhaps the heroes could encounter traps which a thief could disarm or even capture.  Alas, none of this is the case.  Thunderstone is Dominion except that you have to kill your VP cards, which is to say that you have to buy them with the game's second currency (attack) instead of gold.  It's a good game, which is a bit of a shame, because there was the potential for it to be great.  I'm still looking forward to the game that will truly take the Dominion deck building system to the next level.

RATING: 7
MG
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game actually does something with the Dominion mechanics. Instead of simply buying cards, you could instead go kill a monster. Killing monsters gets you the points, but you need to build a good deck with stuff from the village as well. That's the main decision to make on each turn - buy something or kill something. Since the killing can be strictly calculated (and it is almost always better to net points when you can) , you might be able to argue that there is no decision to be made at all, but that would be cynical.

The decisions come into play when you go to the village - do you buy heroes, weapons, light, spells, and so on? The problem with the game is simply that the village cards are not that interesting. You are also limited to one purchase per turn, which often leaves you with a small pile of unspent cash. This is a game that was made for the expansions to liven things up, and boy does this game need them! On the plus side, I could see that a few good expansions could make a big difference.

UPDATE: Thunderstone Advance is slightly better, making this version extinct.
