RATING: 4
This is a step in the right direction for the Dominion concept. It's still based on the fundamental idea that your hand represents buying power, but at least here there are multiple currencies / considerations. The theme is better developed. It takes twice as long as Dominion but I don't mind. 

I'd like to see a well-themed Dominion style game with hands that went beyond buying power, but maybe that's coming. 

Update: after a second play, I want to like it but find myself terribly bored by the pacing and put off by the dead, procedural nature of it. 

2nd Update: I'm not sure this is a step in the right direction for Dominion. Instead I think it shows how deeply unsatisfying Dominion's core mechanics and focus are. There's pretty much nothing about this I like. Not the theme, not the interaction, not the optimization, not the juggling of three purchasing powers. I'm not sure what this game is trying to capture or distill, but I'd rather just play a real game about character setups, party organization, battle tactics, money management, etc.

RATING: 8
Rating based on one play.

I'm somewhat undecided on this one.  It isn't as balanced as Dominion, not by a long shot.  The same information/opportunities is not the same for each player each turn.  There were times when I would have been able to kill a monster but someone beat me to the punch.  Then when it was my turn the current available monsters weren't ones I could beat.  For the first half of the game I had bad luck which in turn made it impossible for me to win.

However, this game has a lot going for it.  The art work is great!  I would pick the art for Thunderstone over Dominion any day.  The theme is much better and is implemented better as well.  By contrast,dominion has one of the most tact on themes I've ever seen in a game.  The fun factor is higher in Thunderstone; It's exciting to level your heros up and see new monsters.  I get a better sense of progress with this game than I do Dominion.

So, I think Dominion as a whole is more balanced and is a more rewarding experience overall.  But Thunderstone can hold its own and I will gladly play again.

Update: I bumped the rating up to an 8.  Despite the luck factor of what monsters are available to fight when it's your turn (a factor that has caused me to lose every time) I keep comping back for more.  The game is more fun, has better art, and a better ambiance than dominion.  However, it still has prove itself to me before I claim this is the better game.

RATING: 5
I can see why some people would like this better than Dominion, as the monster/fantasy theme makes sense and is appealing to many.

This actually makes the game less attractive to me (though I don't necessarily care for Dominion's theme either), but the real spoiler here is the increased length of time it takes to play this compared to Dominion.  Perhaps this is an unfair way to evaluate it, but as the game is obviously inspired by Dominion, I think it is adequate to say that it is a little bloated.

It also feels more difficult to manage your deck.  There are few ways to churn through it, trashing cards is a pretty wasteful process, and after a few turns money is really no object.  This makes it feel like you are more dependent than ever on your hand that you draw each time.  I like that many of the monster cards help you (as opposed to the VP cards in Dominion), but it does seem that timing is very important, as the big point monsters are essential to have any hope of winning.  It also seems pretty powerful to get the Thunderstone.

Still, it is an entertaining alternative to Dominion that I wouldn't mind playing more to see if my opinion changes.

RATING: 6
Thunderstone can't avoid comparison to Dominion. It's much easier to start playing Dominion as to start Thunderstone. The concept of doing an action and buying a card with bought cards breaking these rules is in the end surprisingly lot simpler than choosing between fighting in dungeon or buying a card as you'll have to keep in mind lots more of minor details and the cards also tend to have more information in them. Biggest problem is that monsters aren't stacked in any way that often leads to breaking the flow of the game.

RATING: 9
The Good: Plays pretty well solo - but you have to avoid using certain cards and that is a bummer. Multiple strategies to pick from each game makes one want to play just one more time. Card dividers are included - a nice touch. The implementation of the theme is just so-so for me, but the game-play makes up for it. Overall, I like this a lot more than Dominion.

The Bad: Takes longer than I hoped. My first solo game clocked in at 1 hour and 45 min. That did not include setup or teardown which is a bit of a chore with this game. Although I liked that card dividers were included, they were not very practical in actual use. I understand the expansion has solved this problem and for the base game as well. I am eager to get it. Should include solo rules in the box (there are some posted on BGG)

The Ugly: The rules were very poor in the area of helping a new player identify the cards. And there are TONS of cards in this game. Trying to sort thru the cards and figure out what is what is not for the faint of heart. In addition, the icons are difficult to understand. If you can find someone to teach you this game, it will help you avoid this Ugly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In general, I prefer the theme to dominion. If makes more sense for me that either you accumulate stuff at the village to beef up your deck or either adventure yourself in the dungeon.

One of the thing that makes thunderstone better is that each card has a double function (combat or gold). So you do not end up with dead cards in your hand like with the estate card in dominion

Now the problem with this game is that there are a few annoying things that does not make the game that good and compelling to play again.

First, the iconography and the placement of the value on the cards are very confusion. It's very hard to identify what is what and you are always searching information on the card. Some information are on the text box, so some times you need to move your cards in your hand to see what you have.

There are monsters of different strength in the deck, but there are no monster sorting. So we end up in a situation where a strong monster showed up first making sure that nobody could kill it and forcing player to simply beef up their deck. Personally, they should sort the monster strength.

Another suggestion for dungeon exploration is the lack of surprise. I would have preferred a game with 3 deck of cards for each level and when you explore, you select your level and draw a card. This way, you do not know if you are actually going to win. Entering a dungeon should be scary, if you see what you are going to fight, that makes the feeling completely go away.

When you defeat a monsters you gain EXP card that has the only function of being spend for level up. I don't find this very elegant. I would have either used tokens or a track instead of cards. Else, I would have made that each monster in your hand are worth XP that you can use for level up.

Leveling up heroes is hard and useless. Because player can buy leveled up heroes and prevent up from leveling up your cards because there are no more leveled up cards in play. So it's a bit annoying to give you all the trouble to level up your character when you can simply buy it at an higher level.

Resting is very weak. Losing 1 turn to discard 1 card is very a weak action and should not be done at all. I suggest either allowing player to discard 2 cards or multiple cards from their hands.

The way the game ends is also not that interesting. Of course the game has to end, but I would prefer having something like a quest (which could change on each game). Players would need to go deep in the dungeon to find or do something. When it's done the game ends. 

So again it's the idea of "do I think I am strong enough to do it?" which is a feeling completely missing from the game that should have been placed in priority.

RATING: 6
[u]Comments:[/u]
[b]Game of the Year - 2009[/b]

[u]Comments:[/u]
I have only played the solo version so far, and I definitely want to play this with opponents. I think I much prefer Arcana for the direct player interaction the game offers, but this game is definitely interesting. The card combinations can be fun, and the game presents you with some difficult choices. The variability in the game is just great, and something that I wish other games had. Overall another great game from AEG. 

[u]Comments Update:[/u]
After playing many many many games of Thunderstone I have to say that it is the best Deck Building Game out there that I have played. While I enjoy Arcana more, I would classify that more as a trick tacking game with a deck building facade. Thunderstone has continued to impress with me with each expansion, and with each new expansion I find more reasons why I love the game. Thunderstone still can't be be beat for solo play, but the multiplayer game is just as much fun, if not more fun. I even won the Thunderstone tournament at Gencon 2011. 

[u]Comments Update Update:[/u]
Stick a fork in Thunderstone, and all other deck building games for that matter. Too repetitive, too boring, and to be honest I am just sick of them. Thunderstone was definitely my absolute favorite of the lot, but the whole genre really has been spoiled for me. Sorry Thunderstone, we have had some good times, you and I, but you just aren't what I am looking for anymore in a game. It's not you, really, it's me. I hope we can still be friends.

RATING: 7
Deck building game where you are acquiring adventurers, weapons, spells, etc to then go to the Dungeon and defeat monsters for points.  Very run game with a lot of interesting choices and big improvement on Dominion.  

The only issue with the same is that it can stall.  There can be monsters which are so tough to defeat in combination, you have to get an almost perfect hand to be successful.  You are constantly resting to get the perfect hand.  But this happens occasionally and for the most part this is pretty fun.  

Thunderstone Advance is the better game, but this can be played with it.  Artwork is better in this version than the Advance game.

RATING: 6.8
My playgroup calls this "Dominion with a purpose". Some of us actually find it to be more fun than Dominion due to the thematic elements and because it seems much harder to fall far behind as a result of synergistic combos. Players tend to score points in smaller increments and so I've found that players rarely feel as if they've "already lost" halfway through the game. Unlike Dominion the base set has few mechanics to promote interactivity between players, but the expansions overcome this minor quibble. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
In our (okay, my) quest to find something that my wife and I can play and that scales up for more, we (okay, I) decided to try something different. We'd been moving away from board games with cubes and tokens towards more card-heavy games: At the [i]Gates of Loyang[/i] to [i]Jambo[/i] to [i]Glory to Rome[/i]. So what about one of those deck-building games?  Well after a number of games with my wife, [i]Thunderstone[/i]'s got a pass from the both of us. There could be a little more interaction between players - at this point, it's just discard one card if the other player takes a 2nd or 3rd level thief into the dungeon. Not a real stab-in-the-back kind of move. I'm sure our decks get too bloated with low level cards - but we'd rather buy a new weapon and level up than "waste" a turn resting. It's not a high tension game - we don't count each other's victory points as they go into our decks so neither of us have a good idea of who is winning and how close it is. In fact, the game is quite cooperative as we help each other optimize our hands...although now if one of us declares that they are going to the dungeon, they aren't allowed to change your mind when we discover that they miscounted their attack or light factors. All in all, this game works as a two player game and it works with a third player as well.

RATING: N/A
*R*

House Rules: 

* Monster on top of Thunderstone. (Must resolve monster before acquiring Thunderstone. If monster is returned to bottom of dungeon deck, the Thunderstone accompanies it.)

* Trophy effects on monsters apply to monster in dungeon. (Gryphon has +1 magic attack trophy. Gryphon's attack is 7 +1 magic attack + Battle requirements.)

----

General thoughts: Good mechanic. Easy to lengthen game by increasing number of monsters. Curious to play modules. When that occurs, will comment.

RATING: 5
Thunderstone clearly and undeniably rips off Dominion, which need not have been a bad thing. Indeed, some of the innovations it introduces, like the level advancement of heroes and the decoupled gold/attack currency show the promise of a well-done Dungeoncrawl Dominion. Even the clunkiness of the light mechanic would have been forgiveable, and I'm enough of a fantasy buff that I'd happily play some more couples of rounds of Thunderstone, provided that there's not a copy of Dominion nearby.

Where it fails, and fails hugely, is in its theme and atmosphere, the one area that it certainly should have improved upon its parent game. It begins with the central question of any adventure game that wants to be atmospheric: Who am I? I'm clearly not one of the adventuring heroes, since they are there for me to hire and equip. On the other hand, I can throw some mean fireballs all on my own without any help. Where's the sense in this? Is this meant to portray a world where questing heroes have some kind of tour manager with back-up Pyromancy skills?

This lack of consistent theming, along with the abysmal iconography, will make this game a sure dud with anyone not intimately acquainted with all the fantasy clichés from RPGs and (especially) computer games. In summary, Thunderstone is a merely good game on the shoulders of a great game, and that is just not good enough.

Played once.

Thunderstone does take longer to play than Dominion, though.

RATING: 8
I didn't think I would like Thunderstone when it first came out.  It sounded like the kind of thing my wife wouldn't like (the way monsters work) and I already had a bunch of Dominion languishing (although I like Dominion a lot too).  Anyhow, I finally picked up the ipad version of Thunderstone and WOW! It's really fun! =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Game in a Nutshell: A deck-building game of gathering a party of monster fighting heroes, weapons, and spells then take them into the dungeon to slay the biggest monsters.

Played as: 2 player (Yucata.de only)

Pros: The game does such a good job of theme integration, especially given its very un-thematic main mechanic (deck-building). I like the transition from preparing a party in the village to getting in the dungeon to see how it stacks up. Being able to level-up your Heroes is a personal favorite point. At first I thought there should be a tiny bit more variety in the base set but it still hasn't grown stale for me in the dozens of plays I've had so far.

Cons: This being the first version of this "system" with so many changes and rule tweaks being made in future versions, it's safe to say this, the original, is less refined: The cat and mouse game for the thunderstone card seems to guarantee an anti-climactic ending; the randomness of the monsters available to fight can lead to some brutally hard beginnings and boring conclusions; and so on. Most of this has been addressed by future releases within the series. What hasn't, is some iconography that is laid out with no apparent structure, rhyme or reason and all the "count this, add that, now double, subtract, factor out for another..." stuff that makes it tough not to makes mistakes or oversights (which I still do too frequently after 20+ games). 

Other: Having not played a physical copy of the game, I wonder if things would start to drag on when there isn't a computer doing the book-keeping for quicker reference. Also, I've not played any expansions or later releases, so I can't really vouch for how this game compares to future changes beyond what I know of the rules.

Best Use: A deck-building game for those who complain they lack theme.

RATING: 6
Yes, it's Dominion the Dungeon Crawl.  What you get is a game that has a much more defined theme but less decisions to make overall.  The decks tend not interact with each other too much and it can seem a bit stiff because you're really only usually doing one thing on your turn (attacking, fighting or purging).  All the card movement in Dominion makes it look like you're doing so much more but the reality is that you're usually just spewing cards til you can purchase something. Dominion has more deck-building, you really need a solid engine to compete.  That being said it is a different game and with a few plays under your belt you can begin to see some of its merits.  I would say many of the low ratings are due to people who don't really understand the underlying strategies.  Enjoy playing and each new expansion seems to add more flexibility and challenge.  I also like how the clock works in Thunderstone -- you can't be too patient as you really need to get in there eventually and start defeating monsters, to gain the XP to power your deck further. 2012-09-12

RATING: 3
I don't get it. It's Dominion, plus monsters, minus the smooth engine. Experience, leveling, familiars, monsters, light, all of these mechanisms feel tacked on to make it feel like a dungeon crawl ("thematic parity") as opposed to enhance gameply. You can't build an effective engine because there aren't enough of any of the useful cards to create consistent draws. Taking an experience strategy? Sure, you earned that XP but your level 1 dude can't level up because somewhat bought the last level 2. What?

And the tactical decisions? If you draw money, you go to the village. If you draw attack, you go to the dungeon. You attack the highest monster you can beat. Auto-pilot, go take a nap. Any chance your attack might fail? Only if you fail single-digit arithmetic. Any tension? No.

There are lots of interesting uses of deckbuilding out there. I'm not a Dominion purist. But this game *is* Dominion with a theme makeover that left it like a plastic surgery gone wrong. There's nothing attractive here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
US
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is extremely similar to Dominion (and came out a year after it, so is likely a copy); players start out with a deck of identical cards, and then use those cards to either buy new cards or attack monsters (to get victory points).  Unlike in dominion, there is actually fighting in this game: players use the six cards in their hands (heroes, weapons, magic spells, and accessories) to build a party to storm the dungeon, trying to kill one of three monsters currently available to slay.

There are many combinations players can attempt to build, but there aren't nearly as many chaining combos as Dominion (which is nice).  Players earn victory points for slaying monsters, and also get experience points, with which they can upgrade their fighters.

One thing my group has found odd is that the monsters that come out in the dungeon are not scaled with time.  Thus, at the end of the game, when your deck is finally giving you the combos you want, you may still be fighting a monster that can be killed with your start deck.  And, of course, you can get a super-duper-difficult monster right at the start of the game, which you have no hope of killing.  This leads to a lack of feeling of progress in the game, and some anti-climatic finishes ("oooh, this is the last monster; oh, wait, it only has 2 hit points?  Anyone can kill it!").  

Overall this is an okay game, but the setup time is tremendous (16+ separate piles of cards need to be made), and it doesn't feel as smooth or progressive as Dominion.  However, it lacks Dominion's annoying chaining card combos, which is nice.

RATING: 8

Update 1: Starting to acclimate to the slow pace, patiently building my deck more judiciously. Getting rid of a card while "resting" now and then is far from a wasted turn. I still wish you could routinely buy more than one thing per turn in the village. Also, the solo game (especially the second, "warrior" level) is nicely challenging and works well. Not sure the traps and guardian add much more than randomness to the game; need to try them more (only one guardian in the Wrath expansion--disappointing and a bit chintzy). The best thing about the expansion however is the new, smaller box with well-designed dividers for the base game and Wrath expansion and plenty of room for future expansions.

Update 2: Bumped my rating up a point for how well the  solo option works; very satisfying. Also for some nice extras (treasures!) in the Doomgate Legion expansion.  [51 plays: 8 two-player, 43 solo]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.7
主題:1
主題強烈的RPG式DOMI型卡牌遊戲，套用練英雄升級及地下城打怪的電動玩具模式，來進行domi類團體對抗系統的遊戲，主題還蠻能切合遊戲過程，例如進村莊補給，進地下城打怪。

美術:0.7
卡牌精美，有形形色色的英雄人物，各式各樣的怪物及大魔頭，看本遊戲的卡牌也是一種享受遊戲的過程。不過美術表現上也只能呈現在卡牌上，本項分數也無法滿分。

規則:0.7
規則如同一般domi類型遊戲，先拿12張基本手牌，在遊玩過程中藉由進村莊購買手牌來建立自己的牌庫，進地下城打怪拿分數，規則合情合理沒有太多瑕疵，不過最遊戲中最令人麻煩的還是結算打怪的戰力計算，計算本回合的戰力過程中會令人感覺疲憊，又要加力量持武器，又要加照明度加減燈光懲罰，有時光計算戰力的時間，又讓其他等的玩家覺得不耐煩，不像皇輿爭霸那樣來的令人直觀好計算。  

設置:0.3 
   這次本遊戲最令我詬病之處，domi類的遊戲最麻煩的地方就是擺設卡片，始祖皇輿爭霸已經夠麻煩了，後起之秀雷霆之石不惶多讓更加麻煩好幾倍。
   皇輿爭霸的擺設說實在，玩久熟了都還好，金幣3種、行動卡10種，分數卡3種基本上都很好擺設。雷霆之石還要設定地下城牌庫，先挑出三種怪，有時要加入陷阱卡、守護者卡，洗成地下城牌庫。這是麻煩一。
   皇輿爭霸的行動卡10種挑好就可以擺設，雷霆之石的行動卡，還要細分成英雄四種類型，每一個英雄牌組還要照1.2.3等級排好，另外村莊又分物品兩種武器兩種法術2種，村民傭兵3種。光將村莊牌庫組好，又要花一大段時間，難怪遊戲一直不好推廣，因為擺設過程就嚇跑一堆人，比規則還難搞懂!

路線:0.8
   遊戲過程中，要依據地下城怪物的特性及村莊的英雄武器法術類別，來組成最佳化的個人團隊，路線不可謂不夠廣。本遊戲充滿RPG元素，例如要走騎士，還是法師或是盜匪...等。例外有許多大大小小的擴充，絕對讓本遊戲充滿變化。

互動:0.4
   本遊戲的玩家互動，也是令我覺得詬病的地方，基本上我還比較喜歡將這個遊戲當作單機遊戲來玩，多人場很遺憾沒有玩到幾場，所以評分上會比較主觀一點。本遊戲系統就是玩家一起對抗地下城怪物，在打怪的過程中，看看誰比較神勇打的怪比較多比較高分，過程中偶有一些行動牌可以玩家互相對抗，但也僅僅是雞肋。玩家真能感受到互動的感覺還真的不多。
   此外，個別玩家的牌庫組成，因為面對的怪物及村莊行動牌都一樣，常常會變成我做甚麼，他就做甚麼的情形產生，不像皇輿爭霸一樣，每個人面對的是其他玩家不是遊戲系統，玩家的互動真的比較直接。另外打法上每個玩家的取捨點也比雷霆之石多一點。
   
個人:0.9
   本遊戲最令我欣賞的一點就是單人可以打很爽的桌遊。遊戲規則也很多變化，外加擴充多，不同的怪物模組，對抗不同性質的英雄，都可以讓我在單人遊戲中享受打怪的樂趣，唯一要扣分的是設置太複雜，讓我開的機會比不上皇輿爭霸。

朋友:0.5
   玩不多次，但朋友也覺得沒有皇輿爭霸有趣，我猜跟打怪算分有點複雜有關。畢竟每打怪一次就等於解數學習題一題一樣煩人。

想玩:0.6
   最近單人遊戲有越收越多的趨勢，可能會先開別款，不過有時想練功打怪享受被怪物虐殺的快感時，會想開一下這款，不過面對惱人的設置作業，又會打退堂鼓。

收藏:0.8
   做為我收過的第二款domi類遊戲，有著與眾不同的電玩風格，加上又是中文版遊戲，後續又有一堆中文化擴充，對於這個坑，還是跳了下去，本遊戲還是有它的樂趣存在，尤其是做為單人遊戲。 

總分:6.7

講評:
打完分數後，對於這款大作，我的評分竟然只有6.7分，我自己也嚇了一跳。本遊戲出的擴充不比皇輿爭霸少，我開的次數卻少於皇輿。推究其原因，不是遊戲不好玩，而是設置太麻煩，計算打怪過程太複雜，都是讓人望之卻步的地方，但要是有愛的話，本遊戲倒不失為一款有趣的遊戲。

RATING: 7
Love the artwork, brilliantly produced card game.  Got the version 1.4 rules so no complaints there really.  Fun solo too.  So good it makes me want to try Dominion out!

One of the best deck-builders available, although I don't think I'll be jumping on the expansion band wagon with this.  Also takes a long time to get all the decks shuffled and set up.

UPDATE 15/02/14:

I love the art style for this game, love it.  I really like the idea of light and its impact on the different levels of the dungeon, but I think it’s poorly executed.  It’s just basic maths at the end of the day, but it detracts a lot from what you’re doing whilst you’re working it out, even after a few plays, and there’s so much adding and subtracting that you really get less of a feel of dungeon exploring, which is ostensibly what this game is.  I hear they’ve tightened this up in the new edition, but I’m not really interested in re-buying the game again.

However, as a light deck builder, this passes the time nicely.  The random setups give tons of replayability, but can deliver some occasionally very odd gaming combos.  This particular session we ended up with only really high cost heroes in the line up, which meant it took us longer to get going, but then we beasted through the dungeons more quickly once we were up and running.  The pace was pretty even, though it felt like Sam was dominating for the most part, levelling up his heroes more often than I was.  But all those extra village visits would be his undoing as I just kept chipping away at all the monsters, and eventually beat him to the Thunderstone for the victory.  Just.  The scores were 47 to 45!

A good solid deck builder, but not the best deck builder…

UPDATE 26/02/16

Okay, time to move on to the trade pile.  A lovely game in its time with gorgeous art that has just been eclipsed by all the other deck builders that leap frogged it since.

RATING: 7
In this game, that borrows heavily from the mechanics of Dominion, a player's starting deck consisits of militia, food, torches and daggers. On their turn, one deals out 6 cards, and can go to the village to purchase more things (fighters, items, weapons, spells) or go to the dungeon to conquer monsters. Defeated monsters give you experience points and victory points. The experience points can be used to upgrade your fighters while in the village, and monsters can clog your hand with disease cards. An interesting mechanic is that the dungeon is three levels deep, and you need light from torches or spells, otherwise it is difficult to conquer monsters. The game ends when the thunderstone card migrates to the top level of the dungeon.

I enjoy the game, though I wish there was a way to equalize the turn order. It can't hurt to go first, so if you are going last and the game ends with the first player, you miss a turn. Other than that, I enjoy all the interesting decisions you have to make, and what cards to purchase to try and defeat monsters, who may not be there when your turn comes around!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Very fun game.  Went from interesting to must-have-it-now when I read the Epic variant.  I had been searching for the best dungeon-crawl game for years and everything else had disappointed me.  This one fills the bill quite well.  A little hard to set-up and take down but I can manage it with my own style of randomizing.  

Unfortunately, my girlfriend doesn't like it.  But I can always play solo or with my nephew.

Update:  
After the arrival of Puzzle Strike, and Legendary, there was less of a reason to keep this behemoth around.  The aforementioned games are deck-builders that play quickly and have a certain elegance in their simplicity. Thunderstone is neither quick or elegant.
 
Thunderstone is a slow, plodding beast.  It is fun at times, but tends to take too long and has turns where you have to stop and count and recount to make sure you can finally beat that monster that has essentially frozen the game. Unfortunately, these stasis-inducing moments, break up the flow, the theme and the fun quite often.

Also, it is not that fun to set-up and the Epic Variant requires a familiarity with the game to make it viable, yet by using it you discard even more strategical weight that the official game proffers.  

I tried to make it my default "dungeon run" game, but even solo, it's not that fun to me.  How can I make a fantasy game a "dungeon run" when there are no armor or shield cards because you only take into account how much gold, light, strength, physical attack and magic attack you have. Besides, the deck-building mechanic lends more to players trying to combo cards than making logical fantasy pairings.

I'm disappointed that I bought all the expansions, but I seriously thought I would have played this game for a very long time.

RATING: 3
The concept of a thematically cohesive Dominion is brilliant. Here it is marred by appallingly poor balance and clunky design choices. Certain cards are wildly over- or under-powered, and the consequent destructive effect on gameplay is beyond the help of any simple house rules that I can devise. Add in the tedious light-penalty system, the random difficulty level of monsters in the dungeon at any particular time, and the disincentives for killing low-VP monsters and the game is something of a wreck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Has the same type of deck-building mechanic as seen in Dominion. There are couple of bigger differences compared to Dominion: 
a) There is no plain money cards in game but rather some cards have money value as "extra". Also monsters (aka point-cards) are not equal but have differences and effects.
b) Your turn is not always the same but you choose either to go dungeon (gather points), go village (buy new cards) or rest (remove 1 card from hand from play).

I like both of these differences quite a bit although the choices b) offers aren't that often tough choices. My biggest complain in Dominion always have been a bit "boring" victory condition as you gather enough money (or equivalent) and then try to gain as many points as you can later to end, usually. 

There is more theme present in Thunderstone, which I like, but it also sometimes can be downside as you need more right stuff in hand to gain points. You basically need enough fighters + weapons and preferably some light-cards too in order to hack down bigger monsters deeper fron dungeon. I actually like quite a bit of the mechanic that you only have 3 monsters visible and to fight the ones "deeper in dungeon" you need more light (or suffer some minuses to damage ). That said this is also probably the biggest downside of the game especially with 4+ players. As the amount of monsters doesn't scale with number of players there is less monsters/player when played with more players. That means that if you only get 2 unlucky turns at wrong times you might miss the biggest points/monsters.

Funny that in this game too cards suffer from bad visual design (they wouldn't need to copy this from Dominion!). Here it means symbols that aren't as good as they could be. Perhaps biggest of them being too similar experience points / victory point values on monsters. Probably not as big deal once you know game better but it could be better done.

Best with 3 players but even as with 4+ it feels to have too much luck on how the monster come out and especially the last few since the +3 bonus for slaying the last one seems to be quite a bit of chunk of points.

RATING: 5
Thunderstone is like Dominion but not as good. Primarily because it is longer, more random and has less interaction. The game is more complex and would take longer to learn

I didn't like the random deck of monsters, but I suppose with experience you'd learn what is in each deck. The reason I say it is more random is that there are more conditions that must be met to have a good hand. You need light, people, items, enough strength to use items, enough attack power, and the attack must be of the right type to hurt the monster (magic/non magic/either). If any one of those things fails, you won't be able to kill the monster (though you can work it out before deciding whether to attack or not). But if you keep on drawing hands that put you 1-2 attack power short, you'll lose, like I did.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An interesting Dominion clone with better story and substance, but with the downside potential of bogging down rather than accelerating to an end. Instead of aiming to buy more money and then buy VPs, here you buy weapon and hero cards which have both money and attack points. Depending on what hand you draw on your turn (ie lots of money or lots of attack), you buy better things and add 'em to your deck or you use your attack points to claim a monster card for VPs (also adding it to your now more clogged up deck). The amount of light points on your cards will dictate how far along the monster draft you can attack, and your heroes also may need strength points to be able to include weapon cards in your attack, so there's more things to check, manipulate and manage. As always, deck stripper powers are powerful. The potential bogginess depends on how the monsters come out vs how people have built their decks, with the potential of there being some near unbeatables and people therefore just doing buys rather than progressing the monster kill (which drives the end-game). Once you throw in expansion sets, obviously the game has a ton of variety and replayability. Your preference for this may come down to your tolerance of variable game length and perhaps a more uneven progression through the game - or call it improved storyline if you will.

RATING: 5
The game starts out somewhat like Dominion, but quickly you realize that building you deck to amass large amounts of gold won't work so well.  While the higher-cost town items are pretty good, they'll disappear quickly leaving you with a large pile of gold and nothing good to spend it on.  What you really want to do is buy stuff that will specifically help you fight the monster classes that are available, and thus gain VPs and experience.  The problems with this are twofold.  First, unless you're very familiar with all the monster classes and town items, it's not clear what you should be buying, and you might be stuck with a bunch of useless stuff later.  Second, certain combinations of town cards and monster classes won't work so well, as none of the town cards might be effective against some monsters.  Which is, you know, very frustrating.

(5+ plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's Dominion in a dungeon... it's Dunginion! 

Thunderstone uses Dominion's trademark deck building mechanic in a dungeon theme. 

Players are trying to assemble a party, equip it, and then explore a monster infested dungeon in search of a legendary Thunderstone. On their turn they can rest (trash one card), go to the village to buy items, learn spells or recruit heroes, or take their chances at the monster infested dungeon. Killing monster provides points, experience to the heroes and bonus effects. Experience can be used to upgrade de heroes into more powerful versions.

It's a little more layered than Dominion. It doesn't seem to suffer from those annoying 10 minute turns, where someone plays 20 cards but it also is significantly longer.


The theme works really well and I'm not even all that into D&D or Magic: the gathering. 

Really enjoying this right now.

RATING: 6
All plays for Thunderstone filed under the base game entry.

Main problem with Thunderstone is that the game really starts to drag when your deck clogs up with monsters. 

Also, the new prepare action only helps to a certain extent as you still have to pass quite a few turns before being able to finally draw a good hand. However, I would always play with the new action and never play without it again.

What I like about Thunderstone are the hand management aspects, long-term planning on which cards to get in your deck versus which cards to trash.. In short, all the elements of a good deckbuilder. 

It also has the added element of the dungeon and certain timing issues which Dominion does not have.

RATING: 7.5
pl
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A more relaxed Dominion. Nowhere near as elegant, tense, or challenging, but quite possibly just more fun. Building a band of adventurers and equipment, killing monsters and leveling up is great, especially within the deckbuilding genre with its timing choices.

Dominion is the more challenging and better developed game, though. In Dominion, you have to judge the value of cards to buy within the set of available cards and always keeping in mind what the others are buying. What cards are available, and which aren't, has a huge effect on strategy. In Thunderstone there are simply bad cards, that you never want to buy regardless of the set of available cards. So in any given set (of 16) there are only four or so that you might want, regardless of what 'strategy' you're going for. The question becomes then if it's better to buy a good card or get rid of a bad one, and whether you want to defeat the little monster, or wait for the big one (and to judge this, you need to know what's in the monster deck, and get a little lucky, too).

Fun, but thin on strategy and replayability.

RATING: 6
I love the game play here. A great title. 

I'm highly annoyed by the layout, however. Every card has a "Village" function and a "Dungeon" function. Some do something when you fight the card (called a "Battle" function). The icons that tell you what a card does are scattered all around, however. It would have been easy to put one set on one side, and another on the other, with a few battle icons on the bottom, top, or center.

Also, a picture of a gear means, "Use this in the dungeon," and "DUNGEON" means, "Use this in the dungeon." And also no symbol at all means, "Use this in the dungeon," if it's attack power. 

There's a light system where for every light you're missing, you get -2 Attack. This multiplication could have been removed by simply doubling all the light numbers in the game. 

None of this matters once you get the swing of it, but it really could have been easier to learn and teach.

If I ever inherit a fortune from a long-lost childless uncle, I plan to buy the rights to this awesome game and retool its terrible layout choices.

RATING: N/A
One of the silliest modern games I've played, and a game that makes me highly suspect of what boardgame "design" even is. Because this game is more or less Dominion (i.e., designed by someone else) that a second person (inexplicably referred to as a "designer" as well) has added flavor text to. 

In an attempt to take a mechanism and add theme, the coherence has been removed. And I refuse to accept a game as being thematic simply because it has geek-approved nouns, verbs, and artwork. What on earth is thematic about drawing a pile of weapons into your hand but no one to use them? What is thematic about buying weapons for people, only to have those weapons not show up in your hand? What is thematic about saving up your light (one of about 4 functionally identical game currencies you'll be collecting during the game) so that you can venture farther into the dungeon to encounter the monster whose card art clearly shows them to be outdoors? 

I don't care for deckbuilding as it feels like pre-game to me, and I also don't care for how most deckbuilding games reward players for thinning their deck (as in, "The opposite of building a deck"). So yes, I was probably not going to like this. But it doesn't improve on Dominion, does not engage the imagination, and certainly does not join theme and mechanisms in any meaningful way.

RATING: 8
[b]Quick Comment:[/b]
Thunderstone is a card drafting game in the same vein as Dominion.  Whereas Dominion is a soulless abstract exercise in efficiency, Thunderstone actually has a theme and more complex and interesting card interactions.  It also has a graphic design that puts Dominion to shame.  However it still manages to make a few of Dominion's mistakes, namely the lack of player interaction and the feel that the game is playing you.  Its graphic design could have been better.

[b]Review:[/b]
The Thunderstone set up is longer than Dominion's.  You need to choose three random sets of monsters, four random sets of Heroes and eight random sets of Village cards.  The three sets of monsters are shuffled into a Dungeon deck and the Thunderstone card is shuffled in near the bottom to act as a game ending device.  The top three Dungeon cards are turned face up.  The other cards, along with a few standard start cards go in the Village.  Players get a starting deck of food, militia, daggers and torches from which they draw a hand of six cards.

Cards have various kinds of stats on them: Cost, Gold, Weight, Attack, Light, Strength, etc. as well as unique special powers.  On a turn a player can use the Gold values on their cards to buy cards from the village which go into their deck.  Or they can use the Attack, Strength and Light cards to fight one of the face up Dungeon cards which will be replaced by a card from the top of the deck.  The Dungeon cards are monsters of various types.  When defeated they provide experience points and victory points among other rewards.  A defeated monster also goes in a player's deck.

Some of the fun things about Thunderstone:
1.  There are eight sets of Monsters.  The sets include everything from dragons to demons.  Not only is there variation within a set, i.e. not all dragons are the same, there tends to be a "boss" monster that is stronger than the rest.  However the monsters come out in a random order so you might turn all the bosses face up at the beginning.

2.  The Hero cards can level up and, in so doing, gain power.  But you need to defeat monsters in order to get the XP for leveling up.  Heroes are also needed to use weapons.

3.  The Village may not provide the ideal tools for defeating Dungeon monsters.  Sometimes the Dungeon is a cake walk and sometimes its brutal.  Some may view this as a flaw.  I think it adds to the adventure and exploration elements of the game.

Some not so great stuff:
1.  Like Dominion the primary mode of player interaction concerns who can grab up the cool cards first.  Jim Pinto, the game's developer, has stated that they are trying to come up with player vs. player rules.  I think it would be cool if they were to do so by adding Dungeon cards that require players to attack each other.  Thematically it could represented by monsters that force players to fight each other or something as simple as a treasure horde that two (or more) players stumble upon at the same time.

2.  The cards play you.  Players can't directly control what cards are in their hands.  You can buy stuff and put it in your deck.  But then you have to hope that the right stuff comes out at the right time.  In the CCGs I've played, if you only get half of a combo in your hand you typically will have some way to hold on to that card and fish the rest of the combo from you deck.  But in Thunderstone you have to discard your entire hand every turn.  Why?  Sure, there are some cards that you have to play even if you don't want to.  But I'd like to try a variant where players reveal their hand, play the cards they want and have to and take the rest of their cards back into their hand and refill up to six.

3.  The art is beautiful.  The card lay out could have been better.  Having the card type icons in the upper left corner rather than half way down the card would have made separating out the cards much easier.  And why are the attack values listed in the card text rather than included with all the other basic stats along the left side of the card?  It makes tracking your current attack strength a pain.

These negative issues a side, the game allows players to construct their own dungeon delving decks.  Trying to construct the perfect monster killing deck is a blast.  It's great fun trying to race your opponents for the good cards and the big kills.  The game mechanisms actually seem to be saying something rather than being a pure abstract number game.  Thunderstone is not just a great entry into the deck building genre but into the dungeon crawl genre as well.

RATING: 8
A good game, similar in its basics to Dominion. I'd say that Dominion is a cleaner, simpler design, easier to learn and remember, with less fiddly arithmetic. (You may or may not think these are advantages.) Unlike Dominion, Thunderstone has a real theme, and you (mostly) can't just buy victory points, but must win them by defeating monsters. (Same caveat.)

I wasn't originally sure whether it's really necessary to own both games, and felt that Dominion might flourish in our household while Thunderstone would languish. This turned out not to be true at all. After more than a dozen plays of Thunderstone (plus some solitaire games which I don't log) we're still enjoying Thunderstone very much. And yes, we still enjoy and play Dominion too! They feel different in spite of their many similarities.

RATING: 6
Thunderstone borrows heavily from Dominion, a game I really like, but adds a few twists and turns.

Things I enjoy about Thunderstone:
- I love the theme - its a lot of fun to give your Thyrian Squire a flaming sword and have him kill the Gray Ooze.  To me, this is more satisfying than buying a Duchy.
- The artwork on the cards is top notch and fun to look at.
- The game lasts about 30 to 45 minutes - about the perfect length for me.

Things I do not enjoy about Thunderstone:
- The box insert is the worst insert I have ever seen.
- The light mechanic - while a good idea -is slightly clunky in its implementation.  I could see new players getting frustrated with light penalty questions (or the ability of charaters to carry certain weapons as well).
- The Iconography lacks distinction.  Many of the Icons are slightly similar in appearance.  After a few games I don't have many issues anymore, but a more discernable way of laying out the modifiers (perhaps more colorful?) would be a help to teaching the game.
- The theme sits less well with my wife - if you or your gaming partners are not into fantasy, you might want to stay with Dominion.
- The rules can be very unintuitive, and the rulebook has already been updated on AEG's website.  I am surprised by some of the rulings on the forums in regard to the way certain cards work (specifically pawnbroker).
  
End result - I really enjoy Thunderstone and I am glad I added it to my collection, even with its similarities to Dominion. 

Update : AEG has done its best to address the rule questions w/ rules version 1.4.  I think it works well now, though I think the cards could be better balanced.

Update 3/23/10 : After playing Thunderstone a few more times, I am lowering its rating to a 6.75.  Honestly, I am frustrated with cards like the Selurin that are difficult to understand and seem way overpowered.  There is so much potential in this game, but I am not having as much fun playing it as I think I should have.

RATING: 5
My first step into deck building games and a real mixed bag for me. The mechanics are simple and easy to grasp and the solo mode is solid.

Where it lost me is the theme and presentation. The art is fantastic, brilliant even but it is so devoid of character that I could never get immersed in the game. You have this fantastic bit of art, with a generic title and zero flavour text.

It was years later when I played Shadowrun: Crossfire that I realized that was the main issue I had with Thunderstone. In crossfire you have brilliant art, lots of character and interesting text and quotes on the cards so whilst I am not big into deck builders I still enjoy that game more.

For me Thunderstone is a solid game with none of the charm that makes me want to play a game!

"Okay, we're ready to enter the dungeon of arbitrary project goals. We should be able to defeat a project because we hired some heroic engineers, and bought upgraded servers. How many heroic engineers do we have?"

"But we just hired Alice last turn."

"She's not here."

"Where is she?"

"Somewhere in the deck."

"We have three Wallys and an Asok. So... no."

RATING: 6
One of the best deck-builders out there. It offers a lot of advantages over it's counterparts. First and foremost is the feeling of substance, like you're playing a full game of something instead of some 20-minute affair. Also nice is the short turns. No more endless comboing of villages for 4 coins. Just throw them all out, and do something. But my favorite thing is that this is definitely a fantasy game more than a deck-builder. You do have room for strategy and customization, but more than anything you are fighting monsters.

It's a little lumpy in places. A bad mix of cards will slow the game down a lot, and if the monsters are too tough that'll also bog things down. But you can at least fine-tune the mix of cards, and a slow start will usually mean there will be a speedy denouement. I really like Thunderstone a lot, and it's even better with more expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game draws inspiration heavily from Dominion, but adds a dungeon crawl to the mix.  Players have a common pool of a cards (Village cards) to purchase, building a deck.  They draw a hand of 6 cards each turn, and decide to go to the Village to buy more cards, or go into the Dungeon to face one of three monsters.  The goal of the game is to find the Thunderstone buried in the dungeon; this triggers the end of the game.

Players gain XP to level up heroes, and victory points for killing monsters.  In the end the player with the most victory points in his deck wins the game.  This drives the deck building to be one part economic (to recruit heroes and buy equipment) and one part practical (to kill monsters).  Having a focus on killing monsters really gives the game a purpose that Dominion does not.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I am surprised at how much I like this game.  I enjoy the fantasy genre and so that part clicks with me.  I like the different heroes, exploring different card combos, the different kinds of monsters, and everything.  My two boys (ages 11 and 9) really enjoy the game as well, which is an added bonus since it is a game we all enjoy and can play together (unlike some kids' games).  Have the For the Dwarf promo from BGG.  

The randomizers do not work well.  Creation of village card sets needs to be thoughtful and with consideration of the monsters' particular weaknesses and strengths.  Otherwise, the game can bog down greatly.  For example, if you have golems out but have weak heroes and no way to improve their strength other than iron rations, you are not going to be able to kill the golems.  It gets boring fast when that happens.  Thoughtful consideration is necessary for pacing in this game.

RATING: 3
This is a game that takes the ideas of Dominion, and tries to improve on them unsuccessfully. It doesn't solve any of the problems with Dominion, and adds several problems of it's own. The game drags on much longer than Dominion, and you need combinations of cards in order to accomplish anything. The two currencies are badly implemented here, as you can only use one of those currencies in a turn, and you often need a fair amount of a currency to do anything useful. This, combined with the lack of hand management, makes the game far more luck based than Dominion. Many of the monsters have abilities that make them extremely annoying to deal with, and overall the game delivers an unsatisfying experience. 

The variant epic Thunderstone has potential, but I don't want to spend time fine tuning the card distributions and rules when I could be playing better deckbuilding games anyway. 

The addition of hand management in the more recent sets could help marginally, but not enough to fix the game for me.

RATING: 5
I have to say, this game was something of a surprise to me.  Strangely, I had not even heard of the game prior to learning the game.  It became apparent quite quickly, though that this shared the same (ahem) domain of Dominion.  That in itself is a challenging distinction given how well regarded Dominion is.  And make no mistake, I like Dominion...a lot.

Still, as I listened to the rules, I had very high hopes for Thunderstone.  I came into gaming via Dungeons and Dragons when I was much younger.  I still enjoy well down Dungeon Crawls.  This game seemed like it had a lot of potential -- creating and equipping parties, fighting monsters for experience, advancing characters.  Neat Stuff!!!

Unfortunately, the play left me less than impressed.  Problem one seems to be that the deck-building mechanism really does not hold up to 1-1/2 hour games.  Even games of Dominion that last this long are tedious.  Problem two is the dreaded 7 gold problem from Dominion.  There were many turns where I was 1 coin or one strength shy of being able to do something remotely useful.  Granted, the deck trimming mechanism is more useful than we initially applied it, but I don't believe that it is useful enough to overcome this problem.  There is also the question of the really poor iconography in the game.  It would have taken essentially zero effort to enhance this, and despite the designer's protests otherwise, I don't beleive it would have detracted significantly from the artwork.  

For me, though, the biggest problem is that several times while playing I had the thought, "I would rather be playing Dominion" or "I should have been playing Dominion".  It's never good for your game to make the player wish they were playing something else (better).

All that said, there is plenty going on here that I can see being enough for people to like.  The leveling system is interesting, and I really like the idea of light in the game.  Still, despite statements elsewhere, there is only a single path to victory -- buying victory points, uh, killing monsters.  I hope that future plays allow me to elevate my current rating. [12/29/2009]

RATING: 9
Superb dungeon-themed deck building adventure game with all the elements that make Dominion a blast to play over and over again with the added aspects of within game hero leveling and monster combat! Any fan of AD&D will appreciate the art and attention paid to detail for the characters, monsters, weapons, magic and treasures. Though the rules are necessarily a little more cumbersome than a match of the more fast-paced Dominion,it doesn't take too much time investment to master light modifiers and the guardian breach. The Thundermaster randomizer tool at http://asmor.com/scripts/tsrand/ is a great way to quickly select balanced village and dungeon cards.

RATING: 5
Somehow I end up liking and playing this more and more. Firstly I was - ok, better than other deck builders. Then my girlfriend and friends jumped at it and we just played it a lot. 

Why is it good: best MPS I've played so far (sorry RFTG) though it's hard to pinpoint why. I think developers did a great job with theme to gameplay connection and breaking down unintuitive connection of dominion cards into smaller, graspable parts: weapon connects to a hero, bow to archer, some cards are worth money, other less so or not at all and so on. What comes out of it, is a game, that's fairly easy to grasp and just play. Also great for people who can't handle direct conflict, but like theme. 


Why it's bad: in order to get replayibily of RFTG you'll have to invest in at least one base set and 2 expansions.
 
On Advance: Not sure. I don't like the new visual style at all, weapons look downright terrible.

RATING: 6.5
This reminds me of when El Grande came out and everyone was excited to create games that used Kramer's ideas.  Some of them were good rip-offs, others were really lame rip-offs.  

This is a pretty good rip-off of Dominion.  It's not trying to fool anyone, which is nice.  It took Dominion's ideas and what worked best in Munchkin and made a game for people that wanted more from both.  

Because Monsters are worth VPs, Xps, AND Money (plus some occasional super power), I can't help but feel that the decision tree is to defeat the best monster that you can, then if not feel free to buy something. I hope that there is something more to the game.

Simplistic and clunky, but you know you get to kill some stuff and level up.

RATING: 8
Somewhat flawed, but still a joy to play. This is kind of like Dominion with a purpose. Instead of just collecting those VP cards, you have to do more to earn your VP points (mainly, fight monsters)! I've only played the solitaire variant (hard to get this on the table with my non-gamer friends), so I'm judging it mostly on that aspect, but the sessions have been tense, fun, and a real challenge. Sometimes you'll get a random village that will have no shot against the dungeon (one of the big flaws), but not that big of a deal..... just set up a new village and try again. Many of the card combinations are really interesting, and the game just drips with theme. I've invested in all of the first series expansions, so I'll have tons of replay out of this game for many years to come, trying out new village and hero combos against as many dungeon combos. Always a blast to play.

RATING: 9
After game 6:

Epic Thunderstone with Nir, Doron and Hila.

Had a good start and mid game, clearing all the militia and other junk with the Trainer and Sage. Got a couple of good Drarve Fighters and lots of weapons.

Rolling boulders and bar bowls killed 4 of my heroes...

Still I got ~38 points, place 1 or 2.

------------
After game 4:

Great game.

Got the Thunderstone, but Doron beat me by 1 point.
Completely different strategies: Mine - get only big fat monsters (only 6 total, for 26pts). His - get every tiny monster (around 14, for 30 pts).

Need to work on the deck thinning strategy - used the trainer to thin militia, but kept torches and iron rations till the end. Not enough firepower, not enough gold.

Also, did not get enough gold producing cards early, so got stuck without serious buying ability for most of the mid-game.

Need to search for pre-arranged themed 'packs' of monsters/heroes/village, for better synergy.

-------------
After game 3:

A very engaging and interesting game. Throughout the 2.5 hours you are in a complete focus of ever improving and fine tuning your deck, balancing attack, light, money and victory points.

A great pleasure to reach a quite functioning deck - although it seems that there is so much room for improvement here.

Winning with 31 VPs helps, of course, but it was fun throughout the game before we counted VPs.

Strangely the monsters in the deck did not hinder the progress as much. 

---
After game 2:

Very good game - I still suck at it.

RATING: 6.5
A deck-building game very much like Dominion, but with
- a better-integrated theme (dungeon crawl: buy weapons and hire people in the village, then go off and fight the monsters in the dungeon
- longer play time: both setup and game play take signficantly longer
- more complicated rules: more icons on the cards (light, gold value, strength, weight, experience), more stuff to deal with (e.g., have to have enough strength to use a particular weapon)

1 play, 4er: we had big bad monsters come up early, so we played many many turns with very little actually happening (a bunch of village turns). Don't underestimate the value of light, or you'll end up never being able to fight anything (once all the light sources are bought up by other players)!

I think I may like this better than vanilla Dominion, but need to try it again.

RATING: 7.5
I see why people are comparing this to Dominion.  I think it is because they are virtually the same bloody game!  I can’t even understand the argument that this is ‘completely’ different.  The only thing ‘new’ that Thunderstone offers is the theme and a new resource called ATTACK.  The ATTACK resource is how you buy(kill) VP’s (in the form of monsters).  

The addition of that resource does impact strategy quite greatly though.  In Dominion, you are building a deck and for the most part trying to make it so you can purchase provinces.  Well, the building of the deck revolves around the accumulation of money and purchasing.  Well, in Thunderstone. you have to balance your purchasing power with attack power.  You need gold to buy better attacks and you need attacks to buy (kill) monsters.  So it offers an interesting twist.  

I’ve only played twice, so my experience may be skewed, but in conclusion, the comparison to Dominion is not only expected, it would be criminal not to.

RATING: 4
My comment was redacted because the page refreshed.  It was running a little long, anyway.

Part of the problem in this game came from the cards reading one way, but the rules almanac asking you to play another way that seemed illogical.  For example, a dwarf that equips an edged weapon still retains the +2 Attack even if he later loses the weapon due to reduced strength?  It was little discrepancies like this that added up over time and made me unsure of my grip on the rules.  I really have no interest in reading and memorizing the almanac entry for every card to make sure I'm doing it correctly, so I would probably only play again with somebody who was well-versed in the game.

Another part of the problem was the random distribution of the cards during setup.  Although it does increase the replay value to an insane amount, it can create some very unbalanced and unfun games.  Few of the games we played ever took the 45 minutes it states on the box.  Most of the time, we either got a bunch of kickass heroes and village cards and some very easy monsters to demolish, resulting in some quick, unsatisfying 20-30 minute games, or we got a deck of huge, bad monsters and deficient heroes/village cards, resulting in hour and a half slogs as we finally started putting monsters on the bottom of the deck just to move the game along.

RATING: 5
After my first couple of plays, I was delighted with this game, and thought it was better than Dominion. The theme is more appealing, and it was fun seeing the different moster and hero types the first time around. My rating plummeted after a few more plays, though. Aggravation with the poorly written rules contributed to that, as did the fact that the game apparently didn't get the same excellent development and testing that Dominion benefitted from. It doesn't work as well or as consistently.

The biggest problem, though, is that I came to see theme (which I had initially seen as a plus) as a liability. If I want to kill monsters in a dungeon, I'll play Claustrophobia by choice. If not that, I'd prefer Descent, Castle Ravenloft, Dungeon Run or whatever. Even Cave Troll (an area control game) feels closer to the mark than Thunderstone.

My difficulty is that the monsters in Thunderstone pose no risk, so I don't feel like I'm in an adventure. Instead, I'm checking my inventory for light points and power snacks to see whether I should kill something now or make another shopping run at the village first.

No risk means no excitement. When I do fight something, I know in advance that I'll win. I'm not fighting for my life, I'm shoppping for points. This is a dungeon delving game for supply clerks, not for warriors. After 7 plays, I've had more than enough. Traded.

RATING: 7.5
decent deck-building game with great thematic elements.  Runs long for what it is, and needs more card-action.  More player interaction isn't overly necessary but probably wouldn't hurt.  It's nice since I'm burned out on Dominion after playing nearly every lunch hour for the past three months.

The rules are pretty abysmal and really poorly laid-out--the newer rules posted online are a big help in that regard.  The cards are poorly designed for introducing new players, but are plenty adequate once you've played the game a time or two.

If you like fantasy themes and you like the basic mechanics of a deck-building game similar to Dominion, and you can tolerate the luck that can hamper the best-built decks, then this game is a worthy addition.

RATING: 8
LIKE
+ Great use of the deck building mechanic with only 3 choices of action per turn; village, dungeon or rest.
+ The theme is overused (fantasy) but well integrated into the game with light, strength, heroes and monster type, weapons, spells, etc.
+ Great art on the cards and great variability of cards.
DISLIKE
- Graphic design of the card is pretty bad with little indication of what does what. Plus everything is so muddy and dark. I get that it's a vibe there are going for, but it sacrifices a lot of functionality.
- The previously mentioned design graphic design gives the card a learning curve that shouldn't be there on top of dealing with what's offered in the village.
- Some weird combos (like Selurin) that were left in (or not properly tested?).
VERDICT
To me, double the fun that Dominion is with a much more interesting play and replay value. Expansion are easy to get cheaply and renew the game greatly.

RATING: 6.5
[AEG 2009 edition] {Adventure; deck-building, card-drafting; optimal 4p} Thunderstone adopts the system from Dominion.  The twist here is that players play all cards in the hand, rather than selected ones.  It was quite fun for dungeon crawler fans.

Thunderstone has a strong theme.  User interaction is ok and replay value is good.  Luck of draw is not strong.  Players have to make meaningful decisions to get good scores.  In all, it is a nice game.

However, I still traded this away. Firstly, I prefer Dominion more, which is more elegant and original.  Secondly, Thunderstone is too long for what it is.  Thirdly, my friends don't like this much as Dominion.  Finally, I expected that I have to spend a lot to get its expansions for more interesting games.  Giving shelf space is limited, I have to give it up.

The card illustration is so-so.  It may be a personal taste but I hope that they would be better in future expansions.

[Traded away in October 2010]

RATING: 6.5
I like the ideas behind Thunderstone.  I like how everything is useful in different ways so cards aren't usually dead in your hand.  I also really like the art and the idea of killing monsters in a dungeon.  And that's where it stops.

You need so many different cards to do anything and the likelihood of getting them all in your hand is not good.  So many times I had a lot of attack and no torches.  Useless.  Or I'd have low attack and torches, but none of my cards would be worth gold for the village.  And then the monsters!  Sometimes they are strong, sometimes weak.  But then they may have rediculous conditions on how to kill them.  I could go on, but I don't need to.

Great idea, bad execution.  I'll never play this again.  I don't even like Dominion anymore but I'd gladly play that over Thunderturd.

I played it again and things were different this time.  The starting monster pool was picked as a learning group and the cards available were tailored to the monsters.  The idea was to give me an appreciation of the mechanics which was accomplished.

It's still not a favorite, but I'd play it if asked.  I'll up my rating to a 6.5.

RATING: 8.5
Own the complete set including all the promos. Do play it in Epic variant only: all decks are sharing type only but individual cards are all different.

Have kept the original division on card types the same: so that means 2 piles of weapons and 2 piles of spells, all multi-fighters in their non-fighter pile and so on. Have upped the Swarm and Phalanx monster and hero types respectively to make them actually worthwile to fight against/get. 

Other than that, it is a blast. A staple of my gaming group, it hits the table at least once a month to this date (April 2017). And that is saying a lot after 8 (!) years. Can't think of a game that will really dethrone Thunderstone as the fantasy-themed deckbuilding game to go to (perhaps LOTR complete set but missed out on that a couple of auctions ago... but even then it is a big question if that game actually would've gone down better).

RATING: 5.5
It's a nice Fantasy-Dominion (although Dominion is quite the fantasy game by itself with some cards). It doesn't have the same depth or as many layers as there are to the intricacies of Dominion, but after all it is a fun, nicely illustrated Dungeon crawler of a different kind. Like! 

Update: I noticed something I did not like. In Dominion, every now and then you shuffle yourself a hand that is utterly wasteless. But only every now and then. In Thunderstone this happened to me throughout one whole game. I hardly had a single good hand on my turn. Now you could say my deck was screwed, but really: it wasn't that different from my opponent's decks. I put the missing depth to blame. In Thunderstone it's either good or bad, black or white, do or die. And not having the right cards on hand when the right monster's there can take a downward spiral, because up to three players inbetween you and your next turn have the chance to defeat the monsters that would be "right for you". And so on. 

Update part 2: With Ascension: Chronicles of the Godslayer and Dominion around, I don't see any room for Thunderstone. If I want to play something with more in-game depth, Dominion is incontrovertibly the game of choice. And if it's a more simple but thematical approach to this genre I am looking for, Ascension hits that spot better than Thunderstone does.

RATING: 7.5
A tremendous game that I'll play anytime over Dominion.  It takes much longer, but is also much more fun (in my opinion) to play.  Great game.

OK, this is a surprise.  This is the first game in a long time where it seems to me that anything associated with it would be an automatic purchase.  I haven't felt that way since Combat Commander (which I have seriously neglected recently).  

It is longer than Dominion.
It's not as "elegant" (what an overused word).
It's not as clean, or as easy to grasp on first plays.

It orchestrates the feeling of gathering your forces to plunge headlong into a dangerous dungeon.  Most of the time, you don't die (which is kind of odd for a game about fighting monsters), and it really can be a bit mechanical at times.

But every time I play, I want to play again.  And everyone I have introduced it to, including my wife, absolutely loves it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
On the scale of deckbuilding games that I've played, this ranks higher than DC Deckbuilding and Marvel Legendary, but lower than Dominion and Core Worlds.

The game play is interesting because you have to choose between going to town (buying cards) or going to the dungeon (to get victory point cards) on each turn. The dungeon is made up of groups of monster cards that come out in a random order, so you always have to decide the best way to deal with each monster.

I had a couple of issues with the game. For one, it doesn't seem to scale as well as something like Dominion. There are 30 dungeon cards at any player count, so with two players you each end up with around 15 dungeon cards in your deck. Even though some of them offer a small benefit, most of the dungeon cards just take up space in your deck like VP cards in Dominion.

My other issue is that I feel much more in control in Dominion or Core Worlds. Those games usually have plenty of cards that let you draw more cards from your deck, trash cards you don't need, buy extra cards, etc. In Thunderstone, maybe we got a bad mixture of cards, but there weren't many options that let us draw more cards or pull off interesting combos. So if you drew a bad hand you were just stuck with it.

Update: I've played many more deckbuilders since my initial Thunderstone play, and Thunderstone is still near the bottom of the list. I would hope the Thunderstone Advance or Quest makes some large improvements, but I haven't played them at this time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Love this game and it's expansions...will have the rest up soon.

RATING: 6
Chris Farrell named Thunderstone his 2009 Game of the Year, which surprised me because Chris is the person who taught me Thunderstone at BGG.CON and I didn't get the impression that he, or anyone at the table for that matter, thought it was Game of the Year material at the time. Personally, I thought it was okay. I definitely liked Thunderstone more than Dominion, but that's not saying much for me. I liked how it had more of a theme than Dominion, and I liked how VP cards also gave you experience points to upgrade your fighter cards. I also appreciated the tension between attacking a monster to gain VPs and XP versus buying a card to add to your deck, whereas Dominion often lacks that tension (since Provinces are a given). All that being said, I don't really enjoy Dominion very much, so saying that I think Thunderstone is an improved version of Dominion isn't much of a compliment coming from me. I also thought a downside of Thunderstone was the card layout/design, which was unnecessarily confusing. However, the biggest downside of Thunderstone is that it doesn't use a stacked A, B, C shuffle (as in Tikal, Lowenherz, Robber Knights, etc.) for the monsters. So you can have really tough monsters early and really easy ones late, which is silly because everyone is upgrading the strength of their cards/attacks during the game, so you can whip through the monsters late and be stuck early, and a stacked/sorted shuffle seems like an easy/obvious fix. I'd be happy to play Thunderstone again, but am not dying to. Giving it Game of the Year seems a bit ridiculous though given how over-the-top derivative it is. Even if it is an improvement, it's an incremental improvement that simply further justifies throwing out your copy of Dominion, and standing on the shoulders of midgets doesn't qualify you for any awards in my books.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3.5
Definite Dominion knock-off,  with much better theme integration but much LESS interaction.

dual resources needed for dungeon hands (attack + light) are used to buy VP (defeat monsters), and gold is used to buy normal cards. This generally equates to the game being much more random than dominion.  Also, more than half the numbers on the card have no functional symbology to remind you of their function if you play infrequently.

less decisions, less interaction, more random, more theme.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Not just a Dominion clone!  Thunderstone delivers much greater thematic game play.  Also, it offers more rewarding choices.  Do you go to the village and recruit and equip your party or do you venture into the dungeon and face the monsters therein?  The act of leveling up and equipping your heroes ties everything together in a way that Dominion never has.  The art in Thunderstone is much better IMO than Dominion's.  The only knock I have about Thunderstone is the packaging pales compared to Dominion.  Edit: with the first expansion the packaging issues have been solved.  In fact, this is one of the nicest put together games that I own.  

Though the rules as written are fine we play with the Richard Launius/Tom Vasel variant (with a couple of tweaks by myself).  We throw all the cards together in piles.  1 pile of light cards, 1 pile of food cards, 3 piles of weapons cards (separated into blunt, bow and edged), 2 piles of villagers (1 mercenary and 1 regular), 5 piles of heroes (separated by class: archer, cleric, fighter, thief, and wizard), 2 piles of spells and one pile of magic weapons/items, and the regular piles of torch, dagger, food and militia (plus a pile for elite militia).  We set up the dungeon with 40 random monster cards in this order 20 level one with 1 random trap or treasure, 10 level 2 with another random trap or treasure, finally 7 level 3 monsters and 3 guardians with a third trap or treasure. We found we got stuck when the first 2nd level cards come out. By having extra level 1 cards we could get more equipment and level up more heroes before the level 2 cards arrive. Our games were actually quicker with 40 cards than with 30. Our games have been running about 2 hours.  This variant makes a great game epic.

RATING: 7
This game is quite inferior in gameplay to Dominion, but the theme makes it addictive.  I'm always eager to play it, even though I know it's not really that great of a game.

Some cards are unbalanced, and that can be a problem in this game, since the goal doesn't change too much from game to game.  Almost any every dungeon you fight will require a little magic.  Clerics may be useful in some dungeons, but they are never completely necessary.  So, unlike Dominion, you can play every game the same way, and still be in the running for winning.

EDIT: After finding the rules for Epic Thunderstone, I've raised it to a 7.  It would probably be an 8 or 8.5 if it was published with the Epic Thunderstone rules.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Because I know you're expecting it, here's the inevitable comparison to [gameid=36218].  It's more complex, which means its a better game at heart, but it definitely sacrifices length for it.  Core concepts of deckbuilding is neat, and this game puts several different currencies out there.  Attack is the way you get VPs and XP, gold is the way you add cards to your deck, and XP is the way you improve the cards in your deck.  This game is MUCH more thematic than Dominion.  The concepts of light, weapons, and heroes, while overused, did make a lot better connection with the mechanics than +1 buy, +1 draw, etc.  Thunderstone also has a better decision process through the game, as there is more than just planning out your strategy upon seeing the initial card layout.  Still, I'm not quite sure that I like the mechanic enough to offset the length.  My choice for this genre is still [gameid=69789].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

UPDATE: Suffers in comparison with Puzzle Strike and Ascension. It's clunky and ugly-looking, and takes ages.

Deck-building game with lots of card variety in the base set and a clear theme. It's a dungeon delve in a card game.

When we got it, a friend came around and played it four times in quick succession. I promptly bought the expansions.

It's not as mechanically elegant as something like Dominion and takes longer. But it can be more silly and fun, and more of an experience.

The card variety does generate some weird/ugly combinations of equipment, heroes and monsters. We've played games where the heroes haven't seemed strong enough to beat the monsters in the dungeon, or where key equipment has been missing. For example, no magical items to buy in the village and physically-immune monsters. Or a hero that benefits from big equipment, and no weapons available to buy.

If this causes a problem, I'd recommend reshuffling and reselecting village cards. We decided it was a challenge to overcome, and had a go at playing. Made the game more interesting.

RATING: 6
Certainly, games that build on other games can show a lot of improvement on an idea, but in a lot of cases, what happens is that the newer games take a simple, elegant design and add a lot of other mechanisms to it to make it overly fiddly, and unnecessarily complicated.  I've said this about Zooloretto, and I'll also say it about Thunderstone.  That being said, though, Thunderstone isn't really a bad game.  It takes the idea behind Dominion and turns it into something that feels completely different from Dominion.  It expands a player's choices, meaning that it requires a different way of playing the game, and a different strategy, in order to play well.  

The game can sometimes break down, if the weapons and the monsters don't balance out through a random draw of cards.  One two-player game we played took far longer than necessary, because neither of us had anything else we wanted to (or could, really) buy, but neither did we have the right weaponry to take out some of the big monsters.  Though, admittedly, we could have made an attack to bury the monsters, but it seemed counter-productive to do that.

I admire the design, and wouldn't turn down a chance to play it, just because it feels so different.  I still think Dominion gets an edge over it, due to its simplicity, but they're different enough to justify owning both.

2 plays: 2 players
1 play: 3 players
1 play: 4 players
